Permissibility of Hunting All Animals
Establishing the permissibility of hunting both edible and inedible animals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hunting and explain the proof from Quran and reason.

Core Idea
Hunting is permissible for any animal, edible or not, based on general Quranic command.

Hunting is a common activity with various purposes: food, skins, feathers, or pest control. Islamic law provides a clear ruling on its permissibility. This lesson examines the Hanafi position that hunting is generally allowed for all animals, not just those whose meat is edible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is hunting (istiyad) of animals. The ruling given is permissibility (halal/mubah) for both edible and inedible animals.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The passage cites Quran 5:2: 'And when you are lawful, then hunt.' The imperative form is used.
The verse is addressed to those exiting ihram, but the ruling is general.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The command is absolute (mutlaq) without qualification to edible animals. In Hanafi usul, an absolute command indicates permissibility unless there is a qarina (context) indicating obligation. Here the context of permission after restriction shows ibaha.
Also, the word 'sayd' (game) includes all wild animals, as shown by the poetry example.
4
Consider the rational proof
The passage adds that hunting allows benefiting from skin, feathers, hair, or repelling harm, all of which are legitimate purposes in shariah.
5
Confirm cross-madhhab status
This ruling is generally agreed upon (muttafaq alayh); Hanafis, Shafi'is, and others accept it with some restrictions on method.
The dispute is not on permissibility but on conditions (e.g., using tools).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحل اصطياد ما يؤكل لحمه، وما لا يوكل. لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِذَا حَلَلْتُمْ فَاصْطَادُوا (المائدة: (٢) مطلقا من غير قيد بالمأكول؛ إذ الصيد لا يختص بالمأكول، قال الشاعر: صيد الملوك ثعالب وأرانب وإذا ركبت فصيدك الأبطال ولأن الاصطياد سبب الانتفاع بجلده أو ريشه أو شعره، أو لاستدفاع شره، وكل ذلك مشروع.
And permissible is hunting of what edible meat and what not. Due to His statement: 'And when you are lawful, then hunt' (5:2) absolutely without restriction to edible, because hunting is not specific to edible. The poet said: 'The kings' game is foxes and rabbits, and when you ride, your game is the champions.' And because hunting is a means of benefiting from its skin, feathers, or hair, or to repel its harm, and all that is legislated.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage provides a general ruling and proof. It declares that it is permissible to hunt both animals whose meat is edible and those whose meat is not edible. The proof is the Quranic verse (al-Ma'idah 5:2) which commands hunting after leaving ihram without restricting to edible animals. The commentator adds that the term 'sayd' (game) in Arabic does not specifically mean edible meat; it includes all wild animals. He cites a line of poetry where kings hunt foxes and rabbits (not necessarily eaten). The second proof is rational: hunting allows benefiting from the animal's skin, feathers, hair, or removing its harm, and all these purposes are legitimate in Islamic law.

Key Terms
اصطياد (iṣṭiyād): The act of hunting or capturing wild animals.
مأكول اللحم (ma'kul al-lahm): Animal whose meat is considered edible (halal to eat).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary Qur'anic evidence cited for the permissibility of hunting all animals?
A) 'And when you are lawful, then hunt' (5:2)
B) 'Eat of the good things We have provided' (2:172)
C) 'Lawful to you is game from the sea' (5:96)
D) 'And forage for them from the land' (5:2)

Key Takeaway
Hunting all animals is permissible in Hanafi fiqh based on the general Quranic command and the legitimate benefits.

Liability of the Second Hunter for Game Wounded by Another
Ruling on liability with two scenarios and calculation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the liability of a second hunter when two people wound the same game, distinguish between the scenario where the second alone kills and where both contribute or cause is unknown, and calculate the compensation in each case.

Core Idea
Ownership of game is established by ithkhan; a later hunter destroys owned property.

This passage addresses a common hunting scenario: two hunters strike the same game. The first inflicts a serious wound (ithkhan), establishing ownership. The second then kills it. The ruling clarifies the compensation the second owes to the first. Depending on whether the second's shot was the sole cause of death or both contributed, the calculation differs. Understanding this ruling requires careful attention to the conditions of ownership by wounding and the attribution of death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the relevant scenario
Determine whether the second hunter's shot was the sole cause of death (first wound non-lethal) or whether death resulted from both wounds or is unknown. This determines which calculation applies.
The legal presumption: if the first wound was not fatal and the game could have survived, killing is attributed to the second.
2
Apply the basic compensation rule
If the second alone killed, the second pays the full value of the game at the time of his shot, minus the reduction caused by the first wound. This is because the first already owned the game, and the second destroyed that property.
Example: game worth 10, first wound reduces by 2 → second pays 8.
3
Apply the complex calculation for joint causation
If both wounds contributed to death or the cause is unknown, three payments are due: (1) compensation to the first for the reduction from his wound, (2) half the value of the game as wounded by both wounds, and (3) half the value of its meat as if properly slaughtered.
These three liabilities ensure fair distribution of the loss between the two hunters.
4
Trace the legal reasoning
The ruling is based on the principle that ithkhan (serious wound) transfers ownership of the game to the hunter. Thereafter, any destruction of that game by another triggers liability for compensation.
This is a Hanafi view; other schools may require actual capture for ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وضمن الثاني للأول قيمته غير ما نقصت جراحته وحل اصطياد ما يؤكل لحمه، وما لا يوكل. كلها وقت إتلافه أي جراحة الأول وضمن الثاني إلخ أي ضمن جميع قيمة الصيد غير ما نقصته جراحة الأول؛ لأنه أتلف صيدا مملوكا للغير؛ لأنه ملكه بالإثخان فيلزم قيمة ما أتلف وقيمته وقت إتلافه كانت ناقصة بجراحة الأول، فيلزمه ذلك، بيانه: أن الرامي الأول إذا رمى صيدا يساوي عشرة فنقصه درهمين ثم رماه الثاني فنقصه درهمين، ثم مات، يضمن الثاني ثمانية، ويسقط عنه من قيمته درهمان؛ لأن ذلك تلف بجرح الأول، هذا إذا علم أن القتل حصل بالثاني بأن كان الرمي الأول بحال يجوز أن يسلم الصيد منه، والثاني بحال لا يسلم الصيد منه؛ لكون القتل مضافا إلى الثاني، وإن علم أن الموت حصل من الجراحتين أو لم يدر، ضمن الثاني للأول ما نقصته جراحته، ثم يضمن نصف قيمته مجروحا بجراحتين، ثم يضمن نصف قيمة لحمه ذكيا، هكذا في "العيني"، ومثله في "التكملة" و"ملا مسكين" و"الفتح"، ودلائل هذه الضمانات الثلاثة المذكورة في قوله: "وإن علم أن الموت حصل من الجراحتين" مذكورة في "العيني" و"الفتح"، تركناها لطولها، من شاء فليرجع إليها.
And the second is liable to the first for its value minus what his wound reduced it by. All of it at the time of destruction. i.e., the wound of the first. 'And the second is liable, etc.' means he is liable for the entire value of the game minus what the first's wound reduced it; because he destroyed game owned by another; for he owned it by causing a serious wound, so he owes the value of what he destroyed, and its value at the time of destruction was decreased by the first's wound, so he owes that. Explanation: if the first shooter shoots game worth ten, reducing it by two dirhams, then the second shoots it, reducing it by two dirhams, then it dies, the second owes eight dirhams, and two dirhams are dropped from its value; because that was lost by the first's wound. This is if it is known that the killing occurred by the second, such that the first shot was such that the game could have survived, and the second shot such that it could not survive, since the killing is attributed to the second. If it is known that death resulted from both wounds, or it is unknown, the second owes the first what his wound reduced, then he owes half its value wounded by two wounds, then he owes half the value of its meat as if slaughtered. This is in al-‘Ayni, and similar in al-Takmila, Mulla Miskin, and al-Fath. The proofs of these three liabilities mentioned in his statement: 'if it is known that death resulted from both wounds' are mentioned in al-‘Ayni and al-Fath; we omitted them due to length; whoever wishes may refer to them.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage discusses the fiqh ruling on liability when two hunters wound the same game animal. It begins by stating the general rule: the second hunter is liable to the first for the full value of the game minus the reduction caused by the first hunter's wound. The reasoning is that the first hunter had already owned the game by inflicting a serious wound (ithkhan), so the second hunter has destroyed property belonging to another. The value at the time of the second hunter's shot is already decreased, so he pays only for the remaining value. The text provides a clear example: if the game was worth ten, the first wound reduces it by two, making its value eight; the second hunter then wounds and kills it, so he owes eight, not ten. This applies when it is certain that the second hunter's shot was fatal. If the first shot was non-lethal and the second was lethal, the killing is attributed to the second. However, if death resulted from both wounds, or is unknown, a different calculation applies: the second hunter first pays the first hunter for the reduction caused by the first wound, then pays half the value of the animal wounded by both wounds, and then pays half the value of its meat as if it had been properly slaughtered. The author notes that these three liabilities and their proofs are detailed in other works and are omitted for brevity.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Liability to compensate for loss or damage to another's property.
إثخان (ithkhan): Inflicting a serious wound on game that makes it impossible to escape, thereby establishing ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example given in the passage, if the game is worth 10 dirhams, the first wound reduces it by 2, and the second wound reduces it by 2, and it is known that the second shot killed it, how much must the second pay?
A) 10 dirhams
B) 8 dirhams
C) 2 dirhams
D) 6 dirhams

Key Takeaway
A second hunter who kills game already wounded by another must compensate the first for the value at the time of his action, with a special calculation if both wounds contributed to death or its cause is unknown.

Permissibility of Hunting Any Animal
Ruling with Quranic proof and practical rationale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hunting inedible animals and identify the Quranic proof and practical rationale from the passage.

Core Idea
Hunting is permitted for any animal because the Quranic command is absolute and hunting serves multiple benefits.

This short passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq tackles a common misconception: that hunting is only allowed for animals whose meat is permissible to eat. The author clarifies that the Quranic verse (al-Maidah 5:2) commanding hunting after ihram is unrestricted, and that hunting has other legitimate purposes such as using the skin, feathers, or fur, or eliminating harm. Let's examine the proof and reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the first phrase: 'وحل اصطياد ما يؤكل لحمه، وما لا يوكل'. What is the legal ruling (hukm) for hunting both edible and inedible animals?
The ruling is permissibility (mubah). The word 'حل' indicates it is legally allowed.
2
Locate the Primary Dalil
The author cites Quran 5:2: 'وَإِذَا حَلَلْتُمْ فَاصْطَادُوا'. Identify the type of proof and its authority.
The Quran is qat'i al-thubut but zanni al-dalala here because the imperative is interpreted for permissibility, not obligation.
3
Analyze the Wajh al-Dalalah
The author emphasizes that the verse is 'مطلقا من غير قيد بالمأكول' (absolute, unrestricted to edible). How does this wording prove the generality?
The imperative 'فاصطادوا' is general ('amm). In Hanafi usul, a general command indicates permissibility unless restricted by evidence.
4
Consider the Rationale (Ta'lil)
The passage also provides a secondary rationale: hunting is a means (سبب) for benefiting from skin, feathers, etc., or repelling harm. Note how this supports the ruling.
This rationale is not a proof but a further justification (istihsan or maslahah). It shows that the Shari'ah considers practical benefits.
5
Apply the Ruling
Based on this, would it be permissible to hunt a snake that is dangerous? A fox for its fur? A bird for its feathers?
All are permissible because the ruling is general and the purposes are legitimate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحل اصطياد ما يؤكل لحمه، وما لا يوكل، لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِذَا حَلَلْتُمْ فَاصْطَادُوا (المائدة: ۲) مطلقا من غير قيد بالمأكول؛ إذ الصيد لا يختص بالمأكول، قال الشاعر: صيد الملوك ثعالب وأرانب وإذا ركبت فصيدك الأبطال ولأن الاصطياد سبب الانتفاع بجلده أو ريشه أو شعره، أو لاستدفاع شره، وكل ذلك مشروع.
And hunting what eats its meat and what does not is permissible, due to His statement most high: 'And when you have completed the rites, then hunt' (al-Maidah 2) absolutely without restriction to edible; for hunting is not specific to edible. The poet said: 'The kings hunt foxes and rabbits, and when you ride, your prey is heroes.' And because hunting is a means of benefiting from its skin, feathers, or fur, or to repel its harm, and all of that is legislated.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage establishes the general permissibility of hunting all animals, whether their flesh is lawful to eat or not. The primary evidence is the general command in the Quran (al-Maidah 5:2) to hunt after the state of ihram ends. The verse is absolute and not restricted to edible animals. The point is that the term 'hunting' in the Arabic language encompasses both edible and inedible creatures, as exemplified by the poet's line that kings hunt both foxes and rabbits. Additionally, hunting serves multiple legitimate purposes: benefiting from the animal's skin, feathers, or fur, or even eliminating a harmful creature. All these are valid objectives that the Shari'ah permits. Thus, it is not necessary that the hunted animal be edible; hunting for other benefits is equally allowed.

Key Terms
صيد (sayd): The act of hunting or the game animal itself; here it refers to the act of hunting any animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary reason hunting is permitted for inedible animals?
A) Because the Quranic verse is unrestricted
B) Because the poet said so
C) Because it is a waste to leave them
D) Because their meat is still beneficial

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits hunting any animal, edible or not, based on the absolute Quranic command and the legitimate purposes of benefit or harm prevention.

The Irrevocability of Agency in Pledge
Explains rulings on agency stipulated in pledge contract.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the irrevocability of an agent appointed in a pledge contract and explain the rationale (talil) based on the pledgee's right.

Core Idea
Agency stipulated in pledge is irrevocable because it becomes an inseparable part of the pledge contract, protecting the pledgee's right.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a critical point in pledge law: when the pledgor appoints an agent to sell the pledge upon maturity, can he later dismiss him? The Hanafi school holds that the agency becomes binding and cannot be revoked by the pledgor alone, because the pledgee's right is attached to it. We will examine the ruling, its legal reasoning, and contrast it with independent agency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is stipulating agency in a pledge contract. The ruling is that the agent is not removed by the pledgor's dismissal.
The agent is called 'wakil' and may be the pledgee, 'adl', or a third party.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The text gives two talil (reasons): (1) the agency becomes a description/right of the pledge, binding like the origin; (2) the pledgee's right attaches to it.
The proof is not a direct textual source but a legal reasoning based on the contract's nature.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Because the agency is a condition in the pledge contract, it inherits the pledge's binding nature (luzum). The pledgee's right prevents unilateral revocation.
This distinguishes it from an independent agency.
4
Note the ikhtilaf with al-Shafi'i
Al-Shafi'i holds that the agency terminates with the death of either pledgor or pledgee. The Hanafi school disagrees, arguing the agency survives because the pledge contract itself is not voided by death.
The thamarah al-khilaf is practical: under Hanafi law the agent can sell after the pledgor's death without court permission; under Shafi'i law a new agent must be appointed.
5
Apply the distinctions from independent agency
The passage lists differences: the agent here can be compelled to sell, the ruling on offspring and blood-money differs, selling for a different debt type is allowed, and the agent may sell if the pledged slave is killed.
These distinctions highlight the special nature of agency in pledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الرهن. الرهن يوضع على يد عدل. فإن شرط في عقد الرهن لم ينعزل بعزله وبموت الراهن والمرتمن، وللوكيل بيعه بغيبة ورثته وتبطل بموت الوكيل ولا يبيعه المرتهن أو الراهن إلا برضاء الآخر. وإن شرطت إلخ يعني إن شرطت الوكالة في عقد الرهن بأن يقول الراهن رهنته على أن يكون فلانا وكيلا ببيع الرهن عند حلول الأجل، فليس للراهن أن يعزله، ولو عزله لم ينعزل بعزله، إلا إذا رضي المرتمن بذلك؛ لأن الوكالة لما شرطت في عقد الرهن صارت وصفا من أوصافه وحقا من حقوقه، فتلزم بلزوم أصله؛ ولأنه تعلق به حق المرتهن، وفي العزل اتواء حقه، وكذا لا ينعزل الوكيل بالعزل الحكمي كموت الموكل وهو الراهن، أو ارتداده، ولحوقه بدار الحرب؛ لأن الرهن لا يبطل بموته؛ لتقدم حق المرتهن على حق الورثة، وكموت المرتهن إلا أن يكون وكيلا وهذا عندنا وعند الشافعي: تبطل بموتهما. واعلم أن هذه الوكالة تخالف الوكالة المفردة، وهي التي لم تذكر في ضمن عقد الرهن من وجوه كما في "الزيلعي"، منها ما ذكره المصنف ومنها: أن الوكيل هنا إذا امتنع عن البيع يجبر عليه بخلاف الوكالة المفردة، ومنها: أن الولد والأرش بخلاف ذي المفردة، ومنها: إذا باع بخلاف جنس الدين كان له أن يصر الدين بخلاف المفردة ومنها: أن الرهن إذا كان عبدا وقتله عبد خطأ فدفع القاتل بالجناية كان لهذا الوكيل أن يبيعه بخلاف المفردة. (ملتقطا من الفتح) بيعه إلخ: أي للوكيل المذكور سواء كان المرتمن أو العدل أو غيرهما بيع الرهن بعد موت الراهن لغيبة الورثة كما كان حال حياته البيع بغير حضرته؛ لأنه لم ينعزل بموت الراهن. (كما مر من رد المحتار) هذا يبيع إلى جنس وتبطل بموت الوكيل إلخ : يعني تبطل الوكالة بموت الوكيل مطلقا سواء كان مرتهنا أو عدلا أو غيرهما، ولا يقوم وارثه ولا وصيه مقامه؛ لأن الوكالة لا تجري فيها الوراثة؛ لأن هذا حق عليه والوراثة إنما تجري فيما له لا فيما عليه، ولأنهما رضيا برأيه لا برأي غيره، لكن الرهن باق بحاله؛ لأن الرهن لو كان في يد المرتهن فمات لم يبطل العقد به فلأن لا يبطل بموت العدل أولى، ولأن التسليط بالبيع أمر زائد فيه فلا يبطل الرهن لبطلانه، فإن أوصى العدل ببيعه لم يجز ؛ لأنه لا يقدر على إقامة غيره مقام نفسه في حال الحياة، فلأن لا يملك بعد الوفاة وقد بطلت وكالته أولى إلا أن يكون الراهن قال في أصل الوكالة وكلتك ببيع الرهن وأجزت لك ما صنعت فيه من شيء فحينئذ يجوز لوصيه بيعه، ولا يجوز لوصيه أن يوصي به إلى ثالث، وعن أبي يوسف إن وصي الوكيل كمضارب مات والمال عروض، قلنا: المضارب يملك التوكيل حيا، فيقوم عنه وصيه ميتا، ولا كذلك يملك بيعه الوكيل. (كذا في الفتح ورد المحتار) ولا يبيعه المرتمن إلخ: يعني لا يملك أحدهما بيع الرهن إلا برضاء الآخر لتعلق حقهما به معا، فلا يملك أحدهما إبطال حق صاحبه بغير إذنه. فتح بتغيير)
Book of Pledge. The pledge is placed in the hand of a trustworthy person. If he stipulated agency in the pledge contract, the agent is not removed by the pledgor's dismissal, nor by the death of the pledgor or the pledgee; and the agent may sell it in the absence of the pledgor's heirs; and the agency becomes void with the death of the agent; and neither the pledgee nor the pledgor may sell it except with the consent of the other. The meaning of 'if stipulated' etc.: that if agency is stipulated in the pledge contract, such that the pledgor says 'I pledge it on condition that so-and-so be the agent to sell the pledge upon maturity', then the pledgor cannot dismiss him, and if he dismisses him, he is not dismissed by his dismissal, unless the pledgee agrees to that; because when agency is stipulated in the pledge contract, it becomes a description of it and a right from its rights, so it becomes binding with the binding of its origin; and because the right of the pledgee attaches to it, and in dismissal there is violation of his right. Likewise, the agent is not removed by a constructive dismissal such as the death of the principal (the pledgor), or his apostasy, or his joining the abode of war; because the pledge is not voided by his death, due to the precedence of the pledgee's right over the heirs' right; and similarly by the death of the pledgee unless he is the agent. This is according to us (Hanafis); and according to al-Shafi'i: it becomes void by the death of both. Know that this agency differs from an independent agency (one not mentioned within the pledge contract) in several aspects as mentioned in al-Zayla'i, among them what the author mentioned, and among them: that the agent here, if he refuses to sell, is compelled to do so, unlike an independent agency; and that the (case of) offspring and compensation (for injury) is different from that of the independent one; and that if he sells for a different type of debt, he may convert the debt (to that type), unlike an independent agency; and that if the pledge is a slave who is killed by another slave by mistake and the killer is surrendered for the crime, this agent may sell him, unlike an independent agency. (Extracted from al-Fath) 'His sale' etc.: meaning the aforementioned agent, whether he is the pledgee or the trustee or anyone else, may sell the pledge after the death of the pledgor in the absence of the heirs, just as during his lifetime he could sell in his absence; because he was not removed by the death of the pledgor. (As previously from Radd al-Muhtar) 'This sells' to 'type' and 'the agency becomes void with the death of the agent' etc.: meaning the agency becomes void with the death of the agent absolutely, whether he is the pledgee, trustee, or anyone else; and neither his heir nor his executor takes his place; because inheritance does not apply to agency; because this is a right upon him, and inheritance only applies to rights in his favor, not against him; and because both parties consented to his judgment, not the judgment of another. But the pledge remains as it was; because if the pledge were in the hand of the pledgee and he died, the contract would not be voided by that, so it is more fitting that it not be voided by the death of the trustee; and because empowerment to sell is an additional matter, so the pledge is not voided by its (the agency's) voidance. If the trustee bequeaths its sale, it is not permissible; because he cannot appoint someone else in his place during his life, so even more so he cannot do so after death when his agency has terminated; unless the pledgor said in the original agency: 'I appoint you to sell the pledge and I permit you to do whatever you want with it'; then his executor may sell it; but the executor may not entrust it to a third party. According to Abu Yusuf: the executor of the agent is like a mudarib who died while the capital was goods; we say: the mudarib can appoint an agent during his life, so his executor stands in his place after death; but it is not so for the agent (to have his executor sell). (As in al-Fath and Radd al-Muhtar) 'Nor may the pledgee sell' etc.: meaning neither of them may sell the pledge except with the consent of the other, because both have rights attached to it together, so neither may void the right of the other without permission. (Fath with modification)
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq outlines the special rulings for agency that is stipulated within a pledge contract. The author begins by stating the foundational rule: the pledge is kept with a trusted person. He then explains that when agency is made a condition of the pledge, the pledgor loses the right to dismiss the agent unilaterally. This is because the agency becomes an integral part (wasf) of the pledge contract, binding like the contract itself. The reason is the pledgee's right that attaches to the agent's authority. The agent remains even after the pledgor's death, apostasy, or departure to enemy territory, since the pledge itself survives the pledgor. The same applies if the pledgee dies (unless the pledgee himself is the agent). Here, the Hanafi position differs from al-Shafi'i, who holds that the agency terminates with the death of either party. The passage then contrasts this agency with an independent agency (al-wikala al-mufrada) in several important respects: (1) the agent in the pledge can be compelled to sell if he refuses, whereas in independent agency he cannot; (2) offspring and blood-money rulings differ; (3) if the agent sells for a different type of debt, he may convert the debt accordingly; (4) if the pledged slave is homicide victim, the agent may sell. Then the commentary explains that the agent may sell after the pledgor's death even in the absence of heirs, because his agency is not voided. The agency is voided by the death of the agent himself, and no one succeeds him (heir or executor) because agency is a personal trust. However, an exception is made if the pledgor gave general authority including the right to delegate; then the agent's executor may sell. But even then, the executor cannot further delegate. Abu Yusuf's view is mentioned analogizing to the mudarib. Finally, neither the pledgor nor pledgee may sell the pledge without the other's consent, as both have interests in it.

Key Terms
الوكالة المفردة (al-wikala al-mufrada): An independent agency contract that is not tied to another contract like a pledge.
العدل (al-'adl): The trustworthy person appointed to hold the pledged property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling regarding the dismissal of an agent whose agency was a condition in a pledge contract, according to the Hanafi school?
A) The pledgor may dismiss the agent at any time.
B) The agent is not removed by the pledgor's dismissal, unless the pledgee agrees.
C) The agent is removed only if both parties consent.
D) The agency is automatically void upon the pledgor's death.

Key Takeaway
Agency stipulated in a pledge contract is irrevocable by the pledgor alone because it becomes an inseparable part of the pledge, binding even after death of the pledgor.

Intentional Killing in Hanafi Fiqh: Definition, Ruling, and Dispute
Defining 'amd and its qisas obligation with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of intentional killing ('amd), explain why qisas is the sole obligatory consequence, and contrast the Hanafi position with that of Imam al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Intentional killing with a cutting tool demands qisas as the only default; diya requires the killer's consent.

This lesson defines 'amd (intentional killing) according to the Hanafi school, detailing the instruments that qualify and the twin consequences of sin and qisas. It then examines the crucial dispute with al-Shafi'i over whether qisas is fixed or optional, grounding the Hanafi view in Quran and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Define 'amd in Hanafi terms
Identify the core elements: intent (qasd), striking a person, with a weapon or similar instrument that separates body parts.
Weapons include sword, knife, pointed wood, stone, shard, fire—anything that cuts or pierces.
2
State the legal consequence
The mu'jib (requirement) is two-fold: sin (ithm) for the killer and qisas ('ayn, definite) unless the guardians pardon.
'Ayn means qisas is the specified obligation, not an option.
3
Note the exception: pardon
If the guardians (awliya') pardon, qisas drops; if they pardon for diya, it requires the killer's consent.
This is the Hanafi position: qisas cannot be exchanged for money without the killer's agreement.
4
Present the Shafi'i view
Al-Shafi'i holds that the obligation is one of two unspecified (qisas or diya), or that qisas is specified but the guardian can opt for diya with consent.
The practical difference: in Hanafi, the guardian cannot unilaterally demand diya.
5
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The proof is dalil naqli: Quran {كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الْقِصَاصُ} and hadith 'al-'amdu qawad'. The command indicates obligation and qisas is the only object.
The Hanafi response to Shafi'i is that qisas is ordained to deter harm, so it cannot be replaced without the killer's consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الجنايات قال: موجب القتل عمدا وهو ما تعمد ضربه بسلاح ونحوه في تفريق الأجزاء. المبتدأ: الإثم والقود عينا إلا أن يعفى من غير. قال المصنف: والعمد: قصد ضرب إنسان بسلاح ونحوه في تفريق الأجزاء. والسلاح: كل آلة جارحة كالسيف والسكين. ونحوه: كالمحدد من الخشب والحجر والليطة والنار. فموجب القتل عمدا الإثم والقود عينا، أي القصاص متعينا، إلا أن يعفى عنه الأولياء. وقال الشافعي: الواجب أحدهما لا بعينه، ويتعين باختيار الولي. وعنه أن الواجب هو القود عينا، لكن للولي حق العدول إلى المال برضا القاتل. ونحن نقول: إن موجبه القصاص؛ لقوله تعالى: {كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الْقِصَاصُ} وقوله عليه السلام: "العمد قود" ولا يكون له المصير إلى المال إلا برضا القاتل؛ لأن القصاص تعين لدفع الضرر، فلا يلزم غيره.
Book of Homicides. He said: The cause of killing intentionally is what he intentionally strikes with a weapon or similar in separating parts. The subject: sin and retaliation specifically, unless pardoned by someone else. The author said: Intentional is intending to strike a person with a weapon or similar in separating parts. The weapon: every cutting tool like sword and knife. Its like: pointed wood, stone, shard, and fire. So the consequence of intentional killing is sin and retaliation specifically, i.e., qisas is definite, unless pardoned by the guardians. Al-Shafi'i said: The obligation is one of the two without specification, and it becomes specified by the choice of the guardian. And from him that the obligation is specifically retaliation, but the guardian has the right to shift to property with the killer's consent. We say: Its cause is retaliation; for the Almighty said: {Retaliation is prescribed for you} and his saying peace be upon him: 'Intentional killing leads to retaliation' and he cannot resort to property except with the killer's consent; because retaliation is specified to repel harm, so something else is not necessary.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling on intentional killing ('amd) in Hanafi fiqh. It opens by defining 'amd as intentionally striking a person with a weapon or similar instrument that separates body parts, such as a sword, knife, pointed wood, stone, shard, or fire. The legal consequence (mu'jib) of such intentional killing is twofold: sin (ithm) and qisas (retaliation) specifically ('aynan), meaning the qisas is the determined legal obligation. The exception is if the legal guardians (awliya') pardon the killer, in which case qisas falls away and if pardon is without compensation, the killer goes free; if with compensation (like diya in a settlement), it is valid. The text then presents the inter-madhhab dispute: Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the obligation is one of two things (qisas or diya) not specified, and the guardian chooses; or in another narration, qisas is specified but the guardian can opt for property with the killer's consent. The Hanafi position, derived from the Quranic verse {كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الْقِصَاصُ} and the hadith 'al-'amdu qawad', is that qisas is the specific obligation, and it cannot be commuted to money compensation except with the full consent of the killer, because qisas is required to deter harm. This underscores the emphasis on qisas as the default in intentional homicide.

Key Terms
عمد ('amd): Intentional killing defined as deliberately striking a person with an instrument that separates body parts, done with intent to kill.
قود (qawad): Qisas (retaliation) as the specific legal consequence of intentional killing.
سلاح (silah): A cutting tool like a sword or knife that typically causes severing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, what is the default consequence of intentional killing with a weapon?
A) The guardian may choose between qisas and diya.
B) Qisas is mandatory and cannot be replaced without the killer's consent.
C) The killer must pay diya only.
D) The killer is subject to tazir punishment.

Key Takeaway
Intentional killing ('amd) in Hanafi law requires qisas as the fixed consequence; diya is only possible with the killer's consent.

Intentional Killing: Definition, Instruments, and Consequences
Defines intentional killing with proofs and disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of intentional killing, identify which instruments trigger qisas, and explain why the Hanafi school does not require kaffarah for 'amd.

Core Idea
Intentional killing demands qisas as a fixed right; no kaffarah is added because the Quran commands only qisas.

The Hanafi school defines intentional killing (qatl al-'amd) primarily by the instrument's capacity to wound or kill, not solely by intent. This lesson unpacks the key ruling: qisas (retaliation) is obligatory and cannot be switched to diyah without the killer's consent. We will also examine the dispute over kaffarah and see why the Hanafis reject it for 'amd.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine that the act under discussion is intentional killing (qatl al-'amd) and that its primary consequence is sin (ithm) and retaliation (qawad/qisas).
2
Locate the dalil
Find the two Quranic verses cited: 4:93 for sin and 2:178 for qisas. These are the primary textual proofs.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the imperative 'kutiba' in 2:178 indicates obligation, and how 'mu'minan muta'ammidan' in 4:93 specifies intentional killing.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Identify two disputes: (1) whether the guardian can choose diyah (Shafi'i, Ahmad, Malik) vs. Hanafi ta'ayyun; (2) whether kaffarah is wajib for 'amd (Shafi'i) vs. Hanafi no.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Understand the Hanafi reasoning: qisas is fixed by text and cannot be altered; adding kaffarah is an impermissible addition to the text. The mufta bihi is the Hanafi view.

لا الكفارة: أي لا تجب الكفارة بقتل العمد، وقال الشافعي: تجب اعتبارا بالخطأ، وبه قال أحمد في رواية، ولنا: قوله تعالى: ﴿وَكُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الْقِصَاصُ فِي الْقَتْلَى وفي إيجابها زيادة على النص، بخلاف قتل الخطأ؛ لأن فيه نصا. رمز الحقائق: ٤٢١/٢]
Book of Homicide. Like the sharpened one. Rulings of homicides. Wood, stone, brick, fire: sin and retaliation specifically unless pardoned from... the reed attached to it. The predicate of the subject i.e. retaliation. Even if he killed him with a heavy iron implement and its like, retaliation is obligatory, as if he struck him with a pillar of brass or lead. Al-Tahawi narrated from the Imam (Abu Hanifa) consideration of wounding in iron and its like. Al-'Ayni said: If he killed him with iron or brass that is not sharpened, like a pillar or a weight, there are two narrations; the more apparent is that it is intentional. And in any case, killing with a lead bullet is intentional because it is of the type of iron, and it wounds, so retaliation is carried out. However, if it does not wound, no retaliation is carried out according to al-Tahawi's narration. (Takmilah, Radd al-Mukhtar) 'Like the sharpened one' etc.: i.e., by carving wood so that it has a sharp edge that cuts with it, and the intended meaning is not that there is iron at its tip as mistakenly thought; because that is the coming issue. (Radd al-Mukhtar) And fire: because it does the work of iron in slaughtering such that if it is placed at the throat and cuts what must be cut in slaughter and blood flows, it is permissible; but if it seals and blood does not flow, it is not permissible. There is no difference in the killing being intentional, warranting retaliation, between burning him with it or throwing him into it, whether he dies that same day or not, if he remains bedridden until death. But if he comes and goes then dies, he is not killed (retaliated) as in al-Khaniyyah. (Fath) Sin and retaliation: predicate of the subject i.e. his statement 'cause of killing' and his statement 'and retaliation' is coordinated with it. Retaliation is qisas, i.e. the cause of intentional killing is sin and retaliation. As for sin, because of His statement, the Exalted, {And whoever kills a believer intentionally} (4:93) to the end of the verse. And as for retaliation, because of His statement, the Exalted, {Prescribed for you is retaliation in cases of killing} (2:178), and what is intended is intentional killing because He prescribed blood money in killing him by mistake in His statement, the Exalted, {And whoever kills a believer by mistake} (4:92) the verse. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:420] From... non... 'specifically' is accusative as a circumstantial qualifier of retaliation, i.e. the cause of intentional killing is retaliation while it is specifically alone, and he excepted from it his statement 'unless pardoned', i.e. retaliation is specific unless pardoned by the guardians or some of them; then the specificity of retaliation falls, and it is not obligatory afterward if the pardon is without compensation. If it is with compensation, what is stipulated by settlement becomes obligatory, not by killing. Al-Shafi'i said: The obligatory is one of them without specification, and it becomes specified by the choice of the guardian. And from him (another narration): The obligatory is retaliation specifically, but the guardian has the right to switch to property with the consent of the killer because the human soul is guaranteed by blood money as evidenced by its obligation in accidental killing. But we know the obligation of retaliation by text, so blood money remains, and he chooses. Ahmad said this, and from Malik two narrations like them. And we say: Its cause is retaliation because of His statement, the Exalted, {Prescribed for you is retaliation} and his statement, upon him, 'Intentional killing is retaliation', and he cannot resort to property except with the consent of the killer because retaliation is fixed to repel harm, so other than it is not necessary. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:420] Except that it is pardoned in the passive voice, i.e. except that the guardian of retaliation pardons or settles for something even if it is the same amount of blood money or more. Settlement is also a pardon, except that it is with compensation. If a Muslim is killed intentionally and he has no guardian, the Imam chooses the more appropriate of taking blood money or execution, and he does not have the right to pardon for free. If the guardian pardons half of the retaliation, the whole falls, and the remainder does not convert into property. (Fath) No expiation: i.e., expiation is not obligatory for intentional killing. Al-Shafi'i said: It is obligatory by analogy with accidental killing, and Ahmad said the same in one narration. Our evidence: His statement, the Exalted, {And prescribed for you is retaliation in cases of killing} and imposing it would be an addition to the text, unlike accidental killing because there is a text for it. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:421]
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof defining intentional killing (qatl al-'amd) and its consequences. It begins by listing instruments that cause intentional killing: sharpened wood, stone, brick, and fire. The key point is that the instrument must be capable of cutting or dismembering (muhaddad). Even heavy iron implements like a brass or lead pillar are considered 'amd if they kill, though there is a narration from al-Tahawi requiring a wound. A lead bullet is considered 'amd because it is iron and wounds, but if it does not wound, qisas is not carried out according to one narration. Fire is treated like iron; if it cuts the throat in slaughter, it is permissible, but if it merely seals the wound, it is not. The passage then states that the cause of intentional killing is sin and retaliation. Sin is proven from Quran 4:93, and retaliation from 2:178. The retaliation is specifically qisas (eye for an eye), not an option for the guardian to choose blood money. This is a point of dispute with al-Shafi'i, who holds that the obligatory is one of two things (qisas or diyah) and the guardian can choose. The Hanafi response is that qisas is fixed to deter harm, so the guardian cannot resort to property without the killer's consent. The passage also discusses pardon: if the guardians forgive, qisas falls; if they forgive with compensation, that becomes obligatory. The Imam chooses the best option if there is no guardian. Finally, the passage addresses expiation (kaffarah): the Hanafi school does not require kaffarah for intentional killing, contrary to al-Shafi'i, because the text commands only qisas, and adding kaffarah would be an addition to the text. Accidental killing has a separate text for kaffarah.

Key Terms
العمد (al-'amd): Intentional killing using an instrument that is sharp or capable of dismembering, or any instrument that is intended to kill.
القود (al-qawad): Retaliation (qisas) for killing, i.e., the killer is executed as the sentence.
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): Expiation for killing, typically freeing a slave or fasting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is true about kaffarah for intentional killing (qatl al-'amd)?
A) It is wajib.
B) It is not wajib.
C) It depends on the weapon.
D) It is wajib only if the killer does not pay diyah.

Key Takeaway
Intentional killing (qatl al-'amd) in the Hanafi school is defined by the instrument's capacity to kill, its consequence is obligatory qisas (not diyah by choice), and no kaffarah is due.

Shibh al-'Amd: Definition and Consequences
Defines shibh al-'amd with intra-madhhab differences and its legal consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of shibh al-'amd, explain the intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and list its legal consequences (sin, kaffarah, heavy diyah, no qisas).

Core Idea
Shibh al-'amd is intentional striking with a non-sharp instrument; it incurs sin, kaffarah, and heavy diyah, but not qisas.

In the previous lesson, we studied intentional murder ('amd) which mandates qisas. Here we examine a middle category: quasi-intentional killing (shibh al-'amd). The Hanafi school defines it based on the instrument used, and understanding this distinction is essential for applying the correct penalties. The passage defines shibh al-'amd, cites a hadith, and outlines consequences, while noting the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two foremost students.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its subject
The passage defines shibh al-'amd as a cause of killing alongside 'amd and khata'. Note that the text is explaining the second of five divisions of killing.
Shibh al-'amd is distinct from 'amd and khata'.
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's definition
Abu Hanifa defines shibh al-'amd as intentionally striking with an instrument that has no sharp edge (ghayr muhaddad), like a stone or stick. The instrument does not dismember body parts.
The key is the instrument's ability to cut or separate.
3
Compare with the Sahibayn's definition
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (along with al-Shafi'i) define shibh al-'amd as using an instrument that does not normally kill, regardless of sharpness. Their focus is on the usual lethality of the instrument.
This creates an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
4
Analyze the hadith evidence
The Prophet said: 'Indeed the killed of khata' al-'amd is the killed of the whip and the stick, and it carries one hundred camels.' This hadith supports the category and shows that the instrument (whip/stick) lacks sharpness.
The hadith does not distinguish between types of sticks, implying all sticks are included.
5
List the legal consequences
The consequences of shibh al-'amd are: sin (ithm), expiation (kaffarah), heavy blood money (diyah mughallazah of 100 camels), and no qisas. However, if the act is repeated, the Imam may execute as siyasah.
No qisas because the instrument resembles accidental killing.

وشبهه إلخ إما مرفوع عطف على قوله : "موجب القتل"، والمضاف إليه محذوف، والتقدير: وموجب شبه القتل عمدا فلما حذف المضاف أقيم هو مقامه، وارتفاعه في الحقيقة بالابتداء.
:
رمز الحقائق: ٤٢١/٢] وإما مجرور عطف على القتل، وهذا بيان القسم الثاني من الأقسام الخمسة. (فتح) بغير ما ذكر [ أي بما ليس بسلاح وما جرى مجراه ملا مسكين] أي شبه العمد أن يتعمد ضرب إنسان بغير ما ذكر من السلاح وما يجري مجراه في تفريق الأجزاء وهو الذي لا حد له من الآلة كالحجر والعصا، وكل شيء ليس له حد يفرق الأجزاء، وهذا عنده، وعندهما وعند الشافعي: هو أن يتعمد الضرب بآلة لا يقتل بمثلها في الغالب كالعصا ،والسوط والحجر واليد؛ لأنه يتقاصر معنى العمد باستعمال آلة صغيرة لا يقتل بها غالبا؛ لأنه يقصد بها غير القتل كالتأديب ونحوه، فكان شبه العمد، ولا يتقاصر باستعمال آلة ملبث؛ لأنه لا يقصد بها إلا القتل كالسيف فكان عمدا موجبا للقود، وقوله عليها: "ألا إن قتيل خطأ العمد قتيل السوط والعصا، وفيه مائة من الإبل" من غير فصل بين العصا والعصا، ولأن معنى العمدية قاصر فيه؛ لكونه آلة غير موضوعة للقتل
ولا مستعملة فيه. (عيني) الإثم والكفارة إلخ: بالرفع خبر لقوله: "وشبهه" وما بينهما معترض والكفارة ودية مغلظة" عطف عليه أي وموجب شبه العمد الإثم؛ لأنه قتل عن قصد منه رمز الحقائق: ٤٢١٠/٢] والكفارة؛ لأنه خطأ نظرا إلى الآلة، فدخل تحت قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَنْ قَتَلَ مُؤْمِنا خَطَانَ) (النساء: ۹۲) الآية، ودية مغلظة؛ لإجماع الصحابة على ذلك. (عيني) لا القود أي ليس موجب شبه العمد القصاص؛ لشبهه بالخطأ نظرا إلى الآلة، إلا أن يتكرر منه فللإمام قتله سياسة. (فتح، ملا مسكين والخطأ إما مرفوع أو مجرور على الوجهين المذكورين في قوله: "وشبهه"، وقوله: "ما جرى مجراه" عطف على "الخطأ"، وخبره ما سيأتي من قوله: "الكفارة والدية على العاقلة"، وهذا هو القسم
الثالث
من الأنواع الخمسة.
يرمي
وهو أن يرمي إلخ: تفسير للخطأ ولما كان الخطأ نوعين: خطأ في الفعل، وخطأ في القصد، بين كلا نوعيه، فقوله: " أن شخصا ظنه صيدا" إلخ تفسير للخطأ في القصد، وإنما كان هذا من قبيل الخطأ في القصد؛ لأنه لم يخطئ في الفعل حيث أصاب ما قصد ،رميه وإنما أخطأ في القصد أي في الظن حيث يظن المسلم حربيا، والآدمي صيدا، وقوله: "أو غرضا فأصاب" إلخ تفسير للخطأ في الفعل فإنه أخطأ في الفعل دون القصد. (فتح، تكملة)
And its like (shibh) etc.: either nominative as a predicate coordinated with his statement 'cause of killing', and the add-to is omitted, so the estimation is: and the cause of quasi-intentional killing is... So when the add-to was omitted, it stood in its place, and its being nominative is actually due to being a subject. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:421] Or genitive as a coordinated on 'killing', and this is an explanation of the second of the five divisions. (Fath) With what was not mentioned [i.e., with what is not a weapon and its like (Mulla Miskin)] i.e., shibh al-'amd is that one intentionally strikes a person with what was not mentioned of weapons and their like in dismembering body parts, and it is that which has no sharp edge from the instrument, like a stone and a stick, and everything that does not have a sharp edge that dismembers body parts. This is his (Abu Hanifa's) view. According to both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and al-Shafi'i: it is that one intentionally strikes with an instrument that does not usually kill with its like, like a stick, whip, stone, and hand. Because the meaning of intentionality is weakened by using a small instrument that does not usually kill; because he intends something other than killing, like discipline and the like, so it becomes shibh al-'amd. And it is not weakened by using a dense instrument because he only intends killing with it, like a sword, so it is 'amd warranting retaliation. And his statement, upon him: 'Indeed the killed of khatta' al-'amd is the killed of the whip and the stick, and it carries one hundred camels' without distinction between stick and stick. And because the meaning of intentionality is deficient in it because it is an instrument not made for killing nor usually used in it. ('Ayni) Sin and expiation etc.: nominative as the predicate of his statement 'and its like' and what is between them is parenthetical. 'And expiation and heavy blood money' is coordinated on it, i.e., the cause of shibh al-'amd is sin, because it is killing with intent from him [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:421], and expiation because it is a mistake considering the instrument, so it falls under His statement, the Exalted, {And whoever kills a believer by mistake} (4:92) the verse, and heavy blood money due to the consensus of the Companions on that. ('Ayni) Not retaliation: i.e., the cause of shibh al-'amd is not qisas because of its resemblance to mistake considering the instrument, unless it is repeated; then the Imam may kill him as a disciplinary measure (siyasah). (Fath, Mulla Miskin) And mistake (khata') is either nominative or genitive on the two ways mentioned in his statement 'and its like', and his statement 'what runs its course' is coordinated on 'mistake', and its predicate is what will come of his statement 'expiation and blood money on the 'aqila', and this is the third of the five types. He shoots: i.e., he shoots etc.: an explanation of mistake. Since mistake is of two types: mistake in action and mistake in intention, he explained both types. So his statement 'that he shoots a person he thought was game' etc. is an explanation of mistake in intention. This is of the type of mistake in intention because he did not err in the action as he hit what he aimed at; he only erred in intention i.e., in the assumption where he thought a Muslim was a warrior and a human was game. And his statement 'or a target and it hit' etc. is an explanation of mistake in action, because he erred in the action without the intention. (Fath, Takmilah)
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage defines shibh al-'amd (quasi-intentional killing) and khata' (accidental killing). Shibh al-'amd is when a person intentionally strikes another with an instrument that is not a weapon and does not have a sharp edge, like a stone or stick. This is Abu Hanifa's view. According to Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and al-Shafi'i, shibh al-'amd is when the instrument used does not normally kill, like a whip or hand, because the intent is discipline, not killing. A hadith is cited: 'The killed of khatta' al-'amd is the killed of the whip and the stick, and it carries one hundred camels.' The consequences of shibh al-'amd are: sin (ithm), expiation (kaffarah), and heavy blood money (diyah mughallazah) — the latter due to consensus of the Companions. Importantly, qisas is not imposed because the instrument resembles accidental killing. However, if the act is repeated, the Imam may execute the killer as a disciplinary measure (siyasah). The passage then explains khata' (mistake). There are two types: mistake in intention (khatta' fi al-qasd) — e.g., shooting a person thought to be game or an enemy — and mistake in action (khatta' fi al-fi'l) — e.g., aiming at a target but hitting a person. Both types incur expiation and blood money on the 'aqila (the killer's clan), as will be explained later.

Key Terms
شبه العمد (shibh al-'amd): Quasi-intentional killing: intentionally striking with an instrument that is not a weapon and has no sharp edge, or does not normally kill.
دية مغلظة (diyah mughallazah): Heavy blood money: 100 camels, imposed for shibh al-'amd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, which of the following is an example of shibh al-'amd (quasi-intentional killing)?
A) Stabbing with a knife
B) Hitting with a stone
C) Shooting with a gun
D) Poisoning a drink

Key Takeaway
Shibh al-'amd is defined by the instrument's lack of sharpness (Abu Hanifa) or lethality (Sahibayn); its consequences include sin, kaffarah, heavy diyah, and no qisas unless repeated.

beginner

Khata' (Accidental Killing): Definitions and Liability
Defines the two types of accidental killing (khata') with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the two types of khata' (error in intention vs error in action) and state the liability for each (expiation and diyah on 'aqila).

Core Idea
Khata' is killing without intent, divided into error in intention and error in action; both require kaffarah and diyah on the 'aqila.

Having covered intentional killing in its two forms ('amd and shibh al-'amd), we now turn to accidental killing (khata'). The passage distinguishes two categories: mistake in the intended target and mistake in the act itself. This classification determines liability and reflects the Hanafi school's precision in allocating responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two types of khata'
The passage explains that khata' is of two types: error in intention (khata' fi al-qasd) and error in action (khata' fi al-fi'l). These are different in nature and example.
The text explicitly states this division.
2
Understand error in intention
Error in intention occurs when the killer aims at what he intends (e.g., shoots game) but it turns out to be a human. He did not err in the act of shooting, but in his assumption about the target.
Example: shooting a person thinking he is an animal or an enemy combatant.
3
Understand error in action
Error in action occurs when the killer aims at a valid target (e.g., a target for practice) but misses and hits a person. He erred in the execution of the act, not in his intention.
Example: aiming at a practice target but hitting a passerby.
4
Identify the liability consequences
The passage states that for both types, the liability is expiation (kaffarah) and blood money (diyah) on the 'aqila (the killer's clan). The exact details are deferred to later in the text.
Unlike shibh al-'amd, there is no sin (ithm) because there is no intent to strike.
5
Contrast with shibh al-'amd
Recall that shibh al-'amd involves an intentional striking with a non-lethal instrument. Khata' involves no intention to strike at all (error in intention) or no intention to hit that particular person (error in action).
This distinction affects the presence of sin and the amount of diyah.

Shibh 'Amd: Definition, Ruling, and Proof
Defines shibh 'amd and states its ruling with proof and ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define shibh 'amd according to the Hanafi school, state its ruling (kaffarah and diyah mughallazah), explain the proof from hadith and Quran, and distinguish the Hanafi position on kaffarah from that of al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Shibh 'amd: intentional strike with a non-lethal tool — sin, kaffarah, heavy diyah, no qisas.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi category of shibh 'amd (quasi-intentional killing), a middle ground between full intent and pure error. The passage defines the act, outlines its unique ruling combining aspects of both 'amd and khata', and provides the scriptural basis. Understanding this category prevents misapplication of qisas or wrongful exemption from kaffarah.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Identify the Hanafi definition of shibh 'amd: intentional striking with an instrument that does not normally sever body parts (e.g., stick, stone). Note the slight difference between Abu Hanifa (no sharp edge) and the Sahibayn (instrument that rarely kills).
Both definitions lead to the same ruling.
2
Extract the ruling components
List the four consequences: sin because of intent, kaffarah because of resemblance to error, heavy diyah (100 camels) on the 'aqilah by ijma', and no qisas because the act is not pure 'amd.
The kaffarah is based on Quran 4:92 (killing by mistake).
3
Analyze the proof
Examine the hadith 'Indeed the killed of khata' al-'amd is killed by whip and stick, in it 100 camels' – this distinguishes between sword (pure 'amd) and stick (shibh 'amd). Also note the Hanafi refutation of Shafi'i: no kaffarah in pure 'amd because qisas is prescribed and adding kaffarah would exceed the text.
Hadith is ahad, zanni in transmission and meaning.
4
Map the ikhtilaf
Identify the Shafi'i view that kaffarah applies also to 'amd, and the Hanafi response: the Quranic text on qisas exhausts the ruling for 'amd, so kaffarah cannot be added by analogy.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf; both schools have evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الجنايات
أحكام الجنايات
لا الكفارة وشبهه وهو أن يتعمد ضربه بغير ما ذكر الإثم، والكفارة ودية أي شبه العمد مغللة على العاقلة لا القود والخطأ وهو أن يرمي شخصا ظنه صيدا أو حربيا، سيأتي الكلام في التغليظ فإذا هو مسلم أو غرضا فأصاب آدميا . الروى يرمي هدفا أي السهم
لا الكفارة: أي لا تجب الكفارة بقتل العمد، وقال الشافعي: تجب اعتبارا بالخطأ، وبه قال أحمد في رواية، ولنا: قوله تعالى: ﴿وَكُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الْقِصَاصُ فِي الْقَتْلَى﴾ وفي إيجابها زيادة على النص، بخلاف قتل الخطأ؛ لأن فيه نصا. [رمزه الحقائق: ٤٢١/٢] وشبهه إلخ إما مرفوع عطف على قوله : "موجب القتل"، والمضاف إليه محذوف، والتقدير: وموجب شبه القتل عمدا فلما حذف المضاف أقيم هو مقامه، وارتفاعه في الحقيقة بالابتداء. [رمزه الحقائق: ٤٢١/٢] وإما مجرور عطف على القتل، وهذا بيان القسم الثاني من الأقسام الخمسة. (فتح) بغير ما ذكر [ أي بما ليس بسلاح وما جرى مجراه ملا مسكين] أي شبه العمد أن يتعمد ضرب إنسان بغير ما ذكر من السلاح وما يجري مجراه في تفريق الأجزاء وهو الذي لا حد له من الآلة كالحجر والعصا، وكل شيء ليس له حد يفرق الأجزاء، وهذا عنده، وعندهما وعند الشافعي: هو أن يتعمد الضرب بآلة لا يقتل بمثلها في الغالب كالعصا ،والسوط والحجر واليد؛ لأنه يتقاصر معنى العمد باستعمال آلة صغيرة لا يقتل بها غالبا؛ لأنه يقصد بها غير القتل كالتأديب ونحوه، فكان شبه العمد، ولا يتقاصر باستعمال آلة ملبث؛ لأنه لا يقصد بها إلا القتل كالسيف فكان عمدا موجبا للقود، وقوله عليه السلام: "ألا إن قتيل خطأ العمد قتيل السوط والعصا، وفيه مائة من الإبل" من غير فصل بين العصا والعصا، ولأن معنى العمدية قاصر فيه؛ لكونه آلة غير موضوعة للقتل ولا مستعملة فيه. (عيني) الإثم والكفارة إلخ: بالرفع خبر لقوله: "وشبهه" وما بينهما معترض والكفارة ودية مغلظة" عطف عليه أي وموجب شبه العمد الإثم؛ لأنه قتل عن قصد منه [رمزه الحقائق: ٤٢١٠/٢] والكفارة؛ لأنه خطأ نظرا إلى الآلة، فدخل تحت قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَنْ قَتَلَ مُؤْمِنا خَطَأً﴾ (النساء: ٩٢) الآية، ودية مغلظة؛ لإجماع الصحابة على ذلك. (عيني) لا القود أي ليس موجب شبه العمد القصاص؛ لشبهه بالخطأ نظرا إلى الآلة، إلا أن يتكرر منه فللإمام قتله سياسة. (فتح، ملا مسكين)
No kaffarah for intentional killing and its like, which is that he intentionally strikes him with other than what was mentioned, sin, kaffarah, and diyah, i.e., shibh 'amd, heavy on the 'aqilah, not qisas. For shibh 'amd: sin because intentional in intent; kaffarah because similar to error in instrument; heavy diyah by ijma'. No qisas because similar to error. Except if repeated: imam may execute politically.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage defines shibh 'amd (quasi-intentional killing) and its ruling. It begins by stating that intentional killing ('amd) does not require kaffarah, contrary to al-Shafi'i who analogizes to error. Hanafi proof: Qur'an prescribes qisas, adding kaffarah would exceed the text. Then defines shibh 'amd as striking with an instrument that does not ordinarily kill (e.g., stick, stone), so the intent is not fully murderous. Ruling: sin, kaffarah, heavy diyah on 'aqilah, no qisas. The kaffarah is based on the error aspect, diyah is heavy by ijma' of the Companions. No qisas because of resemblance to error, but if repeated the imam may execute as discretionary punishment.

Key Terms
شبه العمد (shibh al-'amd): Intentional striking with an instrument not meant to kill, like stick or stone.
دية مغلظة (diyah mughallazah): Heavy blood money: 100 camels, with specific characteristics (more severe than regular diyah).
العاقلة (al-'aqilah): The 'aqilah are the male paternal relatives of the perpetrator who are obligated to pay diyah in cases of khata' or shibh 'amd.
القسامة (al-qasamah): Not directly mentioned, but related: the oath procedure in disputed homicides.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling of kaffarah in shibh 'amd according to the Hanafi school?
A) Kaffarah is wajib because it is similar to error.
B) Kaffarah is not wajib because it is similar to 'amd.
C) Kaffarah is wajib only if death occurs from repeated strikes.
D) Kaffarah is makruh.

Key Takeaway
Shibh 'amd is a distinct category in Hanafi fiqh combining sin and kaffarah with heavy diyah on the 'aqilah, but no qisas, with proof from hadith and Quran.

Khata' and Its Two Types: Error in Intention vs. Action
Defines khata' with its two subtypes and introduces 'ma jara majra al-khata''

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between khata' in intention and khata' in action, and state the definition of ma jara majra al-khata'.

Core Idea
Khata' includes both missing your target and hitting the wrong one; both share the same ruling.

Accidental killing (khata') is not a single concept. The Hanafi jurists divide it into two types: error in the intention (you meant to hit game but hit a person) and error in the action (you aimed at a target but missed and killed someone). Understanding this division is essential because the ruling of kaffarah and diyah applies to both. Additionally, acts that are not truly khata' but resemble it (like a sleeping person rolling over and killing) are treated the same way. This lesson unpacks these definitions from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two subtypes of khata'
The text says khata' is of two types: error in action (fi'l) and error in intention (qasd). Locate the phrases that describe each.
Error in intention: thinking a Muslim is a combatant or a human is game. Error in action: shooting at a target but hitting a person.
2
Understand 'ma jara majra al-khata''
The text gives the example of a sleeping person who rolls over and kills. Explain why this is not real khata' (no intention at all) but is treated as such.
The sleeper is excused like the mistaken person, so the same ruling applies.
3
Link definition to ruling
Note that both subtypes and the 'like' category share the same hukm: kaffarah and diyah on the 'aqilah. This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical outcome).
No difference in liability between the two types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخطأ إما مرفوع أو مجرور على الوجهين المذكورين في قوله: "وشبهه"، وقوله: "ما جرى مجراه" عطف على "الخطأ"، وخبره ما سيأتي من قوله: "الكفارة والدية على العاقلة"، وهذا هو القسم الثالث من الأنواع الخمسة.
وهو أن يرمي إلخ: تفسير للخطأ ولما كان الخطأ نوعين: خطأ في الفعل، وخطأ في القصد، بين كلا نوعيه، فقوله: " أن شخصا ظنه صيدا" إلخ تفسير للخطأ في القصد، وإنما كان هذا من قبيل الخطأ في القصد؛ لأنه لم يخطئ في الفعل حيث أصاب ما قصد رميه وإنما أخطأ في القصد أي في الظن حيث يظن المسلم حربيا، والآدمي صيدا، وقوله: "أو غرضا فأصاب" إلخ تفسير للخطأ في الفعل فإنه أخطأ في الفعل دون القصد. (فتح، تكملة)
وما جرى مجراه هذا هو القسم الرابع من الأنواع الخمسة، وهو عطف على قوله: "والخطأ" وتقديره: وموجب ما يجري مجرى الخطأ، وحكمهما أي الخطأ وما يجري مجراه واحد كما سيأتي. كنائم انقلب إلخ: تفسير لما جرى مجرى الخطأ؛ لأنه ليس بخطأ حقيقة؛ لعدم قصد النائم إلى شيء حتى يصير مخطئا لمقصوده، ولما وجد فعله حقيقة وجب عليه ما أتلفه كفعل الطفل، فجعله كالخطأ؛ لأنه معذور كالمخطئ. [تكملة البحر الرائق: ٦٢/٩] الكفارة إلخ: خبر للمبتدأ أعني قوله والخطأ وما عطف عليه وهو قوله: "وما جرى مجراه" والتقدير وموجب القتل الخطأ وموجب ما جرى مجرى الخطأ الكفارة والدية على العاقلة، وإنما كان حكم المخطئ ما ذكر ؛ لقوله تعالى فيه: ﴿فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُؤْمِنَةٍ وَدِيَةٌ مُسَلَّمَةٌ إِلَى أَهْلِهِ﴾ (النساء: ٩٢) وقد قضى به عمر رضي الله عنه في ثلاث سنين بمحضر من الصحابة، فصار إجماعا، ولا يأثم هنا إثم القتل العمد، ولكن لا يعرى عن الإثم لترك المبالغة في التحرز ؛ لأنه ارتكب المحظور في قتل الخطأ؛ لأنه لو لم يكن محظورا لم يجب فيه شيء من الكفارة والدية؛ لأن فاعل المباح لا يلزمه شيء، وقد وجبت عليه الكفارة، وهي توطئة الإثم. (عيني، تكملة، فتح)
And khata'—either nominative or genitive on the two ways… so-and-so is interpretation for khata'. And what is like it: this is the fourth section of five types, its ruling same as khata'. Like a sleeping person rolling over and killing someone, kaffarah and diyah on 'aqilah. Ruling: kaffarah and diyah on 'aqiyah based on Qur'an 4:92, and 'Umar's qada' over three years with Companions, so ijma'.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage explains accidental killing (khata') and what resembles it (ma jara majra al-khata'). Khata' has two subtypes: error in intention (thinking target is game/enemy) and error in action (missing target). Both require kaffarah and diyah on 'aqilah, based on Quran 4:92 and ijma'. 'Umar enforced this in three years. There is no sin of intentional murder, but minor sin for lack of caution. What resembles khata' (e.g., a sleeping person who rolls over and kills) is treated as khata' because he is excused like the mistaken one, even though there is no actual intent error; the act is real but he is excused, so kaffarah and diyah due.

Key Terms
خطأ (khata'): Accidental killing: either error in intention or action, resulting in death of a protected person.
ما جرى مجرى الخطأ (ma jara majra al-khata'): Acts that resemble accidental killing though not strictly error, e.g., a sleeping person causing death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of khata' in intention?
A) Shooting at a deer but killing a person.
B) Cleaning a gun that discharges and kills someone.
C) A sleeping person rolling over and killing a child.
D) Intentionally shooting an innocent person.

Key Takeaway
Accidental killing (khata') is divided into two types (error in intention and error in action), and acts resembling it share the same ruling: kaffarah and diyah on the 'aqilah.

The Ruling of Khata' and Its Like: Kaffarah and Diyah
States the ruling and proofs for khata' and its like

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of kaffarah and diyah for khata' and its like, cite the Quranic evidence and ijma', and explain the minor sin involved.

Core Idea
Whoever causes death accidentally owes kaffarah and diyah; the 'aqilah pays, and a minor sin remains for lack of caution.

After defining khata', the passage turns to the legal consequences: kaffarah (expiation) and diyah (blood money) are obligatory on the 'aqilah (the male relatives of the killer). The proof for this is clear from the Quran (4:92) and the practice of 'Umar radiAllahu 'anhu, which became an ijma'. Importantly, the text notes that even though the killer did not commit intentional murder, he is not free from sin entirely – there is a minor sin for not taking enough precautions. This lesson examines the ruling and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling components
The text states that for khata' and its like, the ruling is 'al-kaffarah wa al-diyah 'ala al-'aqilah'. Break this down: what are the two obligations, and who pays the diyah?
Kaffarah is a personal obligation on the killer; diyah is on the 'aqilah (extended family/tribe).
2
Analyze the Quranic evidence
The passage quotes Surah al-Nisa' 4:92. Identify the exact wording in the source and explain how it proves the ruling.
The verse orders freeing a believing slave and paying diyah to the family.
3
Examine the ijma' evidence
The text mentions that 'Umar radiAllahu 'anhu implemented this ruling over three years in the presence of the Companions, which established ijma'. Understand why this is considered ijma'.
Ijma' is consensus of the mujtahids; the presence of Companions without objection indicates agreement.
4
Understand the sin aspect
The passage notes that there is no sin of intentional murder ('amd), but there is a minor sin for leaving ta'sif (carefulness). How does the text justify this?
Because if the act were purely permissible, there would be no liability; the existence of kaffarah indicates a prohibited element (lack of caution).

Source Text
