Rulings on Analogous Error and Causation Homicide
Defines two categories of homicide and their legal consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between killing by analogous error and killing by cause, and state the Hanafi rulings (diyah, kaffarah, inheritance) for each, applying the evidence from Quran 4:92 and the criterion of direct causation.

Core Idea
Error and causation differ: direct action triggers kaffarah, indirect cause only diyah on the 'aqila.

In Hanafi homicide law, not all killings without intent are equal. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies the third (analogous error) and fourth (causation) categories, showing how the degree of directness determines whether kaffarah and inheritance deprivation apply. We'll trace the proofs and the Hanafi-Shafi'i disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage defines 'what runs in the course of error' (e.g., a sleeping person rolling onto someone) and 'killing by cause' (e.g., digging a well). Note how they differ from true error and from each other.
The sleeper's action is direct but without intent; the digger's action is indirect.
2
Extract the Hanafi ruling for each
For analogous error: diyah on 'aqila and kaffarah are wajib, and the killer does not inherit. For causation: only diyah on 'aqila, no kaffarah, and the killer inherits. Both incur sin for lack of caution, but causation sin is for the act (digging) not the killing.
Causation is the only category that does not prevent inheritance.
3
Locate the primary proof
The proof for error (and analogous error) is Quran 4:92 (freeing a slave and diyah), supported by the practice of Caliph Umar and consensus. For causation, the absence of directness (mubasharah) negates kaffarah, as the Hanafi principle holds.
Quran 4:92 is qat'i al-thubut but zanni al-dalalah - the imperative indicates obligation.
4
Understand the Shafi'i opposing view
Al-Shafi'i annexes causation killing to error in all rulings, requiring kaffarah and preventing inheritance, because the Lawgiver called the causer a killer.
The Hanafi response: the annexation is only partial (for diyah), not for other rulings.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: In causation killing, the Hanafi school does not require kaffarah and allows the killer to inherit, while the Shafi'i school requires kaffarah and prevents inheritance.
This affects blood money distribution and estate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما جرى مجراه هذا هو القسم الرابع من الأنواع الخمسة، وهو عطف على قوله: "والخطأ" وتقديره: وموجب ما يجري مجرى الخطأ، وحكمهما أي الخطأ وما يجري مجراه واحد كما سيأتي. كنائم انقلب إلخ: تفسير لما جرى مجرى الخطأ؛ لأنه ليس بخطأ حقيقة؛ لعدم قصد النائم إلى شيء حتى يصير مخطئا لمقصوده، ولما وجد فعله حقيقة وجب عليه ما أتلفه كفعل الطفل، فجعله كالخطأ؛ لأنه معذور كالمخطئ. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٦٢/٩] الكفارة إلخ: خبر للمبتدأ أعني قوله والخطأ وما عطف عليه وهو قوله: "وما جرى مجراه" والتقدير وموجب القتل الخطأ وموجب ما جرى مجرى الخطأ الكفارة والدية على العاقلة، وإنما كان حكم المخطئ ما ذكر ؛ لقوله تعالى فيه: ﴿فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُؤْمِنَةٍ وَدِيَةٌ مُسَلَّمَةٌ إِلَى أَهْلِهِ﴾ (النساء: ٩٢) وقد قضى به عمر ه في ثلاث سنين بمحضر من الصحابة، فصار إجماعا، ولا يأثم هنا إثم القتل العمد، ولكن لا يعرى عن الإثم لترك المبالغة في التحرز ؛ لأنه ارتكب المحظور في قتل الخطأ؛ لأنه لو لم يكن محظورا لم يجب فيه شيء من الكفارة والدية؛ لأن فاعل المباح لا يلزمه شيء، وقد وجبت عليه الكفارة، وهي توطئة الإثم. (عيني، تكملة، فتح) والقتل بسبب: [بالجر عطف على القتل أي موجب القتل بسبب إما مرفوع أو مجرور على الوجهين وخبره قوله: "الدية على العاقلة"، وهو القسم الخامس من الأنواع الخمسة. كحافر البئر إلخ: تفسير للقتل بسبب أي القتل بسبب كحافر البئر في غير ملكه وهو مشروط بما إذا كان بغير إذن السلطان، وكذا واضع خشبة على قارعة الطريق ونحو ذلك، إلا إذا مشى على البئر ونحوه بعد علمه بالحفر ونحوه. (فتح، رد المختار) الدية على العاقلة لأنه التلف، سبب وهو متعد فيه بالحفر فجعل كالدافع الملقي فيه فتجب فيه الدية صيانة للأنفس، فتكون على العاقلة؛ لأن القتل بهذا الطريق دون القتل بالخطأ فيكون معذورا، فتجب على العاقلة تخفيفا عنه كما في الخطأ بل أولى؛ لعدم القتل منه مباشرة، ولهذا لا تجب الكفارة فيه تكملة البحر الرائق: ٦٣/٩] وهذا إذا كان الحفر على ممر الناس، وإذا لم يكن لا دية عليه. (تكملة، ملا مسكين) لا الكفارة: أي لا تجب فيه الكفارة؛ لعدم المباشرة. رمز الحقائق: ٤٢٢/٢] ولا يأثم إثم القتل بل إثم الحفر والوضع في غير ملكه. (در مختار) والكل إلخ أي كل نوع من أنواع القتل الذي تقدم يوجب حرمان الإرث إلا القتل بسبب، فإنه لا يوجب ذلك كما لا يوجب الكفارة وقال الشافعي هو ملحق بالخطأ في أحكامه. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٦٣/٩] لأن الشرع أنزله قاتلا ولنا أن القتل معدوم فيه، والحق بالخطأ فبقي في غيره على الأصل. (فتح)
And what runs in its course: this is the fourth division of the five types, and it is connected to his statement: 'and error'; its estimation: and the obligation of what runs in the course of error, and the ruling of both – error and what runs in its course – is one, as will come. Like a sleeping person who rolled onto a man and killed him: explanation of what runs in the course of error because it is not error in reality due to the sleeper's lack of intention toward anything such that he would be mistaken in his aim, and since his action occurred in reality, what he destroyed is incumbent upon him like the action of a child, so it is made like error because he is excused like the mistaken. [Takmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 9:62] The atonement etc.: it is the predicate of the subject, meaning his statement 'error' and what is connected to it, which is his statement 'and what runs in its course'; its estimation: the obligation of killing by error and the obligation of what runs in the course of error is atonement and blood money on the 'aqila. This ruling for the mistaken is as mentioned due to His statement: 'So the freeing of a believing slave and a compensation delivered to his family' (al-Nisa 4:92), and 'Umar decided it over three years in the presence of the Companions, so it became consensus. He does not commit the sin of intentional killing here, but he is not free from sin for leaving off the utmost caution because he committed a prohibited act in killing by error; for if it were not prohibited, nothing of atonement or blood money would be obligatory – because the doer of a permissible act is not liable for anything – yet atonement is obligatory on him, and it is a prelude to sin. ('Ayni, Takmila, Fath) And killing by cause: (by genitive case, connected to 'killing') i.e., the obligation of killing by cause – either raised or declined in two ways, and its predicate is his statement 'blood money on the 'aqila' – and it is the fifth division of the five types. Like one who digs a well, etc.: explanation of killing by cause, i.e., killing by cause like one who digs a well on non-owned land, conditioned upon it being without the ruler's permission; similarly, one who places a log on a public road and the like, except if one walks onto the well etc. after knowledge of the digging etc. (Fath, Radd al-Mukhtar) The blood money is on the 'aqila because the destruction is by a cause, and he transgressed through digging, so he is made like the pusher who throws, so blood money is obligatory for protection of lives; thus it is on the 'aqila because killing in this way is lesser than killing by error, so he is excused; so blood money is on the 'aqila to relieve him, as in error, even more so due to the absence of killing directly from him; hence no atonement is obligatory in it. [Takmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 9:63] This is when the digging is on a public passage; if not, no blood money is on him. (Takmila, Mulla Miskeen) No atonement: meaning atonement is not obligatory in it due to lack of directness. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:422] He does not commit the sin of killing but the sin of digging and placing on non-owned land. (Durr Mukhtar) And all etc.: i.e., every type of killing that preceded necessitates deprivation of inheritance except killing by cause, for it does not necessitate that just as it does not necessitate atonement; and al-Shafi'i said it is annexed to error in its rulings. [Takmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 9:63] Because the Lawgiver considered him a killer; our proof is that killing is non-existent in it, and it was annexed to error, so it remains in other aspects on the original. (Fath)
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on two categories of homicide: (1) what runs in the course of error (ma jara majra al-khata') and (2) killing by cause (qatl bi sabab). The author explains that 'what runs in the course of error' is the fourth of the five types of killing as classified in the matn. It is not true error because the actor lacks intention, but his action is directly causative. The example given is a sleeping person who rolls onto someone and kills them. Such a person is excused like one who errs, and the ruling is the same as error: atonement (kaffarah) and blood money (diyah) on the 'aqila. The proof is drawn from Quran 4:92 and the practice of Caliph Umar, establishing consensus. The sleeper, though not sinful for the killing itself, still incurs sin for lack of caution. The second category, killing by cause, is exemplified by digging a well or placing a stone in a public path without permission, leading to someone's death. Here, only blood money is on the 'aqila, no atonement, because the killer did not directly cause death. The condition for liability is that the act occurs on a public route; otherwise no diyah. Finally, the passage notes that all types of killing (intentional, quasi-intentional, error, and analogous to error) prevent inheritance to the killer, except killing by cause, which does not prevent inheritance just as it does not require atonement. Al-Shafi'i considers causation killing as fully annexed to error in all rulings, but the Hanafi position distinguishes it due to the absence of direct killing. This is a clear example of Hanafi differentiation based on the degree of direct causation.

Key Terms
ما جرى مجراه (ma jara majrahu): Literally 'what runs in its course'; refers to acts not true errors but legally treated like error because the actor is excused (e.g., a sleeping person rolling onto someone).
القتل بسبب (al-qatl bi sabab): Killing by cause, where one performs an act (like digging a well) that indirectly leads to death, without directly causing it.
العاقلة (al-'aqila): The group of male agnatic relatives of the killer who are responsible for paying diyah in cases of accidental or quasi-accidental killing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is NOT a consequence of killing by cause (qatl bi sabab)?
A) Payment of diyah on the 'aqila
B) Obligation of kaffarah
C) Sin for the causative act (e.g., digging)
D) Killer is not deprived of inheritance

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the gradation of homicide categories (intentional, quasi-intentional, error, analogous error, causation) has practical consequences for diyah, kaffarah, inheritance, and sin, with causation being the lightest.

Intentional Wound Leading to Bedridden Death: Qisas Ruling
Ruling on qisas with causal chain analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas for an intentional wound causing bedridden death and identify the two factors that break the causal chain, thereby preventing qisas.

Core Idea
Intentional wound causing death demands qisas unless the victim recovers or the wounder's status breaks causation.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on qisas when a person intentionally wounds another, the victim becomes bedridden (dha firash), and then dies. The ruling turns on whether the wound is an apparent cause of death and whether any intervening factor severs the attribution. We will explore the reasoning and exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
State the specific act (intentional wounding) and the legal consequence (qisas is wajib upon the wounder).
2
Determine the dalil and its nature
The proof is qiyas (analogy): the wound is an apparent cause of death, so death is attributed to the wounder by analogy to direct killing.
The dalil is zanni al-thubut and zanni al-dalala.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
Identify the specific phrase 'sabab zahir' (apparent cause). How does this justify qisas? Because causation is established through the apparent link.
4
Identify the exceptions that break the chain
Two factors cut off attribution: (1) hurr al-raqabah (freedom of the neck – interpreted as the wounder being the owner of a slave victim or the wounder freeing the victim as expiation), and (2) al-buru' minhu (the victim fully recovers from the wound). In either case, qisas falls.
These exceptions show that causation must be uninterrupted.
5
Apply the ruling to a hypothetical
Consider a scenario: A intentionally wounds B, B becomes bedridden and dies after two weeks. No recovery, and A is not B's owner. Qisas is due. If B recovers fully before dying from another cause, no qisas for the wound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن جرح رجلا عمدا فصار ذا فراش ومات يقتص. ومن جرح إلخ: يعني إذا جرح إنسان آخر، فصار المجروح صاحب فراش حتى مات، فإنه يقتص من الجارح؛ لأن الجرح سبب ظاهر لموته، فيحال الموت عليه ما لم يوجد ما يقطعه كحر الرقبة، أو البرء منه. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٧٨/٩]
Whoever wounds a man intentionally and he becomes a bedridden invalid and dies, he is retaliated against. 'Whoever wounds' etc.: meaning if a person wounds another, then the wounded becomes a bedridden invalid until he dies, then retaliation is taken from the wounder because the wound is an apparent cause of his death, so death is attributed to him as long as nothing cutting it off exists, like the freedom of the neck, or healing from it. Completion of al-Bahr al-Raiq: 78/9.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on qisas. The ruling states that if a person intentionally wounds another, and the victim becomes bedridden (dha firash) and then dies, the wounder is subject to qisas. The commentary explains that the wound is considered an apparent cause (sabab zahir) of death; therefore, death is attributed to the wounder unless there is something that breaks the causal chain. Two factors break the chain: (1) 'freedom of the neck' (hurr al-raqabah) — this likely refers to the wounder being the owner of a slave victim, where qisas is not due because of different legal status, or it may refer to a situation where the wounder frees the victim as expiation; (2) the victim fully recovers from the wound (al-buru' minhu). If either occurs, qisas is not imposed because the death is not directly tied to the wound. This is a clear ruling within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
ذا فراش (dha firash): A person who becomes bedridden due to injury, unable to move from bed.
حر الرقبة (hurr al-raqabah): Freedom of the neck; in this context, likely refers to the wounder being the owner of the slave victim, which breaks the causal chain and prevents qisas.
سبب ظاهر (sabab zahir): An apparent cause, i.e., a cause that is clearly linked to the effect in normal circumstances.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, which of the following prevents qisas for an intentional wound causing bedridden death?
A) The victim being a minor
B) The wounder's repentance
C) The victim's full recovery from the wound
D) The victim's family forgiveness

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, intentional wounding causing death through an apparent cause demands qisas unless the causal chain is severed by the victim's recovery or the wounder's ownership status.

Liability for Multiple Causes: The Hanafi Jins Principle
Ruling on apportionment of diyah for multiple causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for apportioning diyah when multiple causes contribute to death and explain how actions are classified into legal types (jins) to determine liability.

Core Idea
Liability for diyah is divided by legal types of actions, not by the number of physical causes.

When a person dies from several simultaneous causes, how do we apportion the blood money? This lesson unpacks a classic Hanafi case where the victim's own action, Zayd's action, a lion's attack, and a snake's bite all contribute. You will learn that the Hanafi school classifies actions into three 'types' (ajnas) based on their legal and moral consideration, and only Zayd's action carries full liability in this world.

Guided Path
1
List all physical causes
Identify every cause that contributed to the death: the victim's own action, Zayd's action, the lion's attack, and the snake's bite.
There are four physical actions, but they are not treated equally.
2
Classify each cause by its legal type (jins)
Determine whether each action is 'fully disregarded' (hadr), 'disregarded in this world but sinful in the next', or 'fully considered' in both worlds.
3
Count the distinct types
Group actions that share the same legal status: the lion and snake are both hadr in this world and the next → one type. The victim's own action is hadr in this world but sinful in the next → second type. Zayd's action is fully considered → third type.
4
Apportion liability
Since there are three legal types, the total diyah is divided into three equal shares. Zayd, whose action is the only one carrying liability in this world, pays one-third.
5
Determine payment source
If Zayd's action was intentional, he pays from his own wealth. If unintentional (shibh 'amd or khata'), the 'aqila pays.
The source of payment depends on the nature of Zayd's act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن مات بفعل نفسه وزيد وأسد وحية، ضمن زيد ثلث الدية. ضمن زيد إلخ: لأن فعل الأسد والحية جنس واحد؛ لكونه هدرا في الدنيا والآخرة، وفعله بنفسه جنس آخر؛ لكونه هدرا في الدنيا معتبرا في الآخرة، حتى يأثم به، وفعل زيد معتبر في الدنيا والآخرة، فصار ثلاثة أجناس: ومعتبر ومعتبر من وجه دون وجه وهو فعله بنفسه، فيكون الثابت فعلا واحدا، فيجب على زيد ثلث الدية، ثم إن كان فعل زيد عمدا تجب عليه الدية في ماله وإلا فعلى العاقلة تكملة البحر الرائق: ٧٨/٩]
Whoever dies by his own action, and the action of Zayd, the action of a lion, and the action of a snake, Zayd is liable for one-third of the blood money. 'Zayd is liable' etc.: because the action of the lion and the snake are one type, since their blood is shed without penalty in this world and the next, and his own action is another type, since it is shed without penalty in this world but considered in the next, so he sins by it, and the action of Zayd is considered in this world and the next, so they become three types: considered, and considered from one aspect but not another which is his own action, so what is established is one action, so Zayd must pay one-third of the blood-money, then if Zayd's action was intentional, he pays the blood-money from his own wealth, otherwise it is upon the 'aqila. Completion of al-Bahr al-Raiq: 78/9.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage deals with a scenario where a person dies due to multiple simultaneous actions: his own self-inflicted harm, the action of Zayd, the attack of a lion, and the bite of a snake. The key question is how liability is apportioned. The commentary explains that the actions are categorized into three 'types' (ajnas) based on their consideration in the legal/moral order: (1) The lion and snake are one type because their actions are legally disregarded (hadr) in both this world and the hereafter—no compensation is due from them, and no sin. (2) The victim's own action is a second type: it is disregarded in this world (no liability) but is sinful in the hereafter. (3) Zayd's action is a third type: it is fully considered in both this world and the hereafter, so he bears liability. Because these three types combine to cause death, the total diyah is divided into three shares, and Zayd must pay one-third. If Zayd's act was intentional, he pays from his own wealth; if it was unintentional (shibh 'amd or khata'), the payment falls on his 'aqila (tribal/agnatic surety). This ruling illustrates the Hanafi principle of dividing liability according to legal categories of actions.

Key Terms
جنس (jins): Type or category of action based on its legal and moral consequences (e.g., hadr, mu'tabar).
هدر (hadr): Blood shed without legal claim for retribution or compensation; disregarded.
العاقلة (al-‘aqilah): The group of male agnatic relatives who pay diyah for unintentional killing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a person dying from his own action, Zayd's action, a lion's attack, and a snake's bite, why is Zayd liable for only one-third of the diyah?
A) Because there are four causes, so each gets one-fourth
B) Because the actions of the lion and snake are lumped together as one type, the victim's own action as a second type, and Zayd's action as a third
C) Because Zayd is only partially responsible
D) Because the victim contributed to his own death

Key Takeaway
Zayd pays one-third diyah because the lion and snake form one type (hadr), his own action a second type, and Zayd's action a third; liability is apportioned by legal types, not number of physical causes.

Killing a Weapon or Stick Brandisher: Conditions for No Liability
Extended ruling on individual brandisher with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which killing a person who brandishes a weapon or stick at you incurs no liability in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Killing a weapon brandisher is always permitted; killing a stick brandisher is permitted only if help (ghawth) is unavailable.

You have learned that brandishing a weapon against Muslims in general makes one's blood licit. Now, the same principle applies when an individual is targeted, but with an important distinction: the type of object and the availability of help matter. This lesson unpacks the specific conditions that determine when defensive killing is without legal consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
Recognize that the passage addresses the case where a person brandishes a weapon or stick at a single individual, and the victim kills the brandisher. The ruling is that no liability (diyah or qisas) falls upon the killer under certain conditions.
The baseline ruling is that killing is permissible (mubah) and blood is hadar.
2
Distinguish Weapon vs. Stick
Note the difference: for a weapon (e.g., sword), killing is allowed regardless of time or place. For a stick, additional time and place restrictions apply because a stick may give the victim time to seek help.
The passage explicitly mentions 'casha' (stick) with conditions, while 'silahan' (weapon) is unconditional.
3
Apply the Time/Place Condition for a Stick
A stick brandished at night in a city, or by day outside a city (on a road or wilderness), makes killing permissible because help (ghawth) cannot be summoned in time. If the stick is brandished by day in a city where help is available, killing would incur liability.
Ghawth is the key determinant: availability of rescue or assistance.
4
Consider the Brandisher’s Intent
The text adds that the circumstances must indicate seriousness, not jest or play. If the situation clearly shows the brandisher is joking, killing is not permitted. However, if there is ambiguity, the default is to treat it as serious.
The principle that intentions are judged by outward signs (zahir al-hal) applies.
5
Understand the Proof
The ruling is based on the hadith 'Whoever brandishes a sword against Muslims, his blood is licit,' extended by analogy to an individual, and on rational reasoning that when help is unavailable, killing is the only means of self-defense.
The proof combines transmitted (naqli) and rational (aqli) evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن شهر على رجل سلاحا ليلا أو نهارا في المصر أو غيره، أو شهر عليه عصا ليلا في مصر، أو نهارا في غيره، فقتله المشهور عليه، فلا شيء عليه، الشاهر أو غيره دفعا عنه المشهور عليه. ومن شهر على رجل إلخ : أفاد بهذه المسألة أن الواحد كالمسلمين في الحكم المذكور سابقا، ولما كان السلاح لا يمهل ، فيحتاج إلى دفعه بالقتل ليلا كان أو نهارا في مصر أو غيره. عصا إلخ: سواء كان صغيرة أو كبيرة؛ لأن العصا الصغيرة وإن كانت تمهل، ولكن في الليل لا يلحقه الغوث، فيضطر إلى دفعه بالقتل، وكذا في النهار في غير المصر في الطريق لا يلحقه الغوث، فإذا قتل كان دمه هدرا. فلا شيء عليه: أي على المشهور عليه بقتل الشاهر لما روينا وبينا من المنقول والمعقول، وهذا إذا لم تدل الحال على المزح واللعب، ولا يعتبر احتمال الجد وإظهار المزح، ولو دل الحال على الجد جاز قتله دفعا. (فتح)
And whoever brandishes a weapon against a man by night or day in the city or elsewhere, or brandishes a stick by night in the city or by day in other than a city, and the one upon whom it is brandished kills him, there is nothing upon him, whether the brandisher or someone else defending him. 'And whoever brandishes' etc. He taught by this issue that the individual is like the Muslims in the previously mentioned ruling, and since a weapon does not give respite, it is necessary to repel him by killing whether by night or day in the city or elsewhere. 'Stick' etc. whether it is small or large, because even a small stick gives respite, but at night he cannot get help, so he is forced to repel by killing, and likewise in the daytime in other than a city on the road, he cannot get help, so if he kills, his blood is shed without liability. 'There is nothing upon him' meaning upon the one upon whom it is brandished for killing the brandisher, for what we have narrated and explained from transmitted and rational proofs, and this is if the situation does not indicate jest and play, and the possibility of seriousness and appearance of jest is not considered, but if the situation indicates seriousness, it is permissible to kill him in defense. (Fath)
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage extends the previous ruling to an individual target. Brandishing a weapon against a single person is treated the same as against the generality of Muslims: the brandisher's blood is licit at any time or place. For a stick (small or large), the ruling is more nuanced: if the stick is brandished at night in a city, or by day outside a city (where help is not readily available), the victim may kill the brandisher without liability. However, if the brandishing occurs by day in a city, where the victim can potentially call for help, killing the brandisher makes one liable (as seen in the next lesson). The commentary also notes that the intent of the brandisher matters: if the circumstances show jest or play, the branch is different; but if the situation is serious, killing is permitted. The proof is a combination of the earlier hadith and rational reasoning that when help is not available, killing is the only means of defense.

Key Terms
الغوث (al-ghawth): Help or rescue that can be summoned, especially from bystanders or authorities.
هدر (hadar): Blood shed without legal consequences (diyah or qisas).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is killing a person who brandishes a stick considered without liability?
A) At any time in a city
B) Only at night in a city or by day outside a city
C) Only if the stick is large
D) Never, a stick is never a lethal threat

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of killing a brandisher depends on the type of weapon and the availability of help (ghawth) at the time and place.

Killing a Brandisher After Withdrawal: Qisas Due
Ruling when a brandisher withdraws after striking, then is killed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a brandisher withdraws after striking and is then killed, and explain the reasoning based on the return of inviolability ('ismah).

Core Idea
Once the attacker withdraws, his inviolability returns; killing him after that is unjust murder requiring qisas.

Self-defense is a well-established right, but it is strictly conditional on the continuation of the threat. This lesson examines a specific scenario from the Hanafi tradition: a person brandishes a weapon, strikes his victim, and then withdraws (insarafa). The question is whether the victim may lawfully kill the brandisher after the withdrawal. The source presents the ruling that the killer is subject to qisas, because the brandisher's blood was only licit during the active threat. Once he desists, his legal protection is restored, making any subsequent killing a capital crime.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Understand the specific facts: a person (the brandisher) brandishes a weapon, strikes the victim, then withdraws (insarafa). After the withdrawal, the victim strikes and kills the brandisher.
The withdrawal must be a genuine cessation of the attack, not a temporary pause.
2
Determine the Initial Legal Status
During the brandishing and striking, the brandisher's blood is licit (halal ad-dam) because he is actively threatening life. This is based on the principle that self-defense permits lethal force against an imminent threat.
The permission is temporary and conditional.
3
Assess the Effect of Withdrawal
When the brandisher withdraws (insiraf), his evil (sharr) is repelled, and the need for defensive killing ends. Consequently, his inviolability ('ismah) returns, making him legally protected like any other innocent person.
The return of 'ismah is immediate upon withdrawal.
4
Apply the Ruling
Since the victim killed after the withdrawal, he killed a protected person unjustly. Therefore, qisas (retributive justice) is obligatory on the killer.
The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ضربه الشاهر فانصرف، فقتله الآخر، قتل القاتل. قتل القاتل: معناه إذا شهر رجل على رجل سلاحا فضربه الشاهر ، فانصرف بعد الضرب، ثم إن المضروب المشهور عليه ضرب الضارب، وهو الشاهر، فقتله، فعليه القصاص؛ لأن الشاهر لما انصرف بعد الضرب عاد معصوما مثل ما كان؛ لأن حل دمه كان باعتبار شهره و ضربه، فإذا رجع على وجه لا يريد ضربه ثانيا اندفع شره، فلا حاجة إلى قتله؛ لارتفاع شره بدونه فعادت ،عصمته، فإذا قتله بعد ذلك فقد قتل رجلا معصوما ظلما، فيجب عليه القصاص تكملة البحر الرائق: ٨١/٩]
And if the brandisher strikes him and then withdraws, and the other kills him, the killer is killed. 'The killer is killed': meaning if a man brandishes a weapon against a man, and the brandisher strikes him, then he withdraws after the strike, then the struck one (the one upon whom it was brandished) strikes the striker (the brandisher) and kills him, then he is subject to qisas, because when the brandisher withdrew after the strike, he became inviolable like he was, because the permissibility of his blood was due to his brandishing and striking, so when he retreated in a way that he does not intend to strike him again, his evil is repelled, so there is no need to kill him, and his inviolability returns, so if he kills him after that, he has killed an inviolable man unjustly, so qisas is obligatory upon him. Completion of al-Bahr al-Raiq: 81/9.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario: a person brandishes a weapon and actually strikes his victim, but then withdraws (i.e., stops the attack and retreats). After the withdrawal, the victim then strikes and kills the brandisher. The ruling is that the victim is subject to qisas. The reasoning: the brandisher's blood was temporarily licit only because of his threat and attack. Once he withdraws and desists, his evil (sharr) is repelled, and the need for lethal defense ends. His inviolability ('ismah) returns. Thus, killing him after withdrawal is an unjust murder, mandating qisas. This highlights that the permission to kill in self-defense is conditional upon the continuation of the threat.

Key Terms
انصرف (insarafa): To withdraw or retreat after an attack, indicating cessation of the threat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a brandisher strikes and then withdraws, what is the ruling on the victim who then kills him?
A) No liability, because the brandisher started the attack
B) Qisas is due
C) Diyah only
D) It is permissible if the brandisher is a criminal

Key Takeaway
The protection of life is restored immediately upon the attacker's withdrawal, making subsequent killing a capital offense.

Killing a Fleeing Thief to Recover Property
Ruling with proof on killing a thief to recover property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on killing a thief in defense of property and identify the condition that justifies lethal force.

Core Idea
You may kill a fleeing thief to recover your property only if you cannot retrieve it otherwise.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a critical scenario: a homeowner whose property has been stolen and whose thief is escaping. The ruling is clear—no liability attaches to the homeowner if he kills the thief while trying to recover the property, provided he has no other means to retrieve it. We will examine the textual proof and the logical analogy that supports this position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is killing a thief who has already taken the property and is fleeing. The ruling is that the homeowner is not liable (no diya or qisas).
Focus on 'fala shay'a alayh' — no liability.
2
Locate the Dalil
The author cites the Prophet's saying about fighting in defense of property (qatil duna malik). This is the primary proof.
The exact wording is 'قاتل دون مالك'.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
The phrase indicates that one who fights (qatal) in defense of property is justified, implying that killing the thief is permissible and incurs no liability.
The hadith is general, covering both initial prevention and recovery.
4
Note the Condition
The passage adds a condition: the owner may kill only if he cannot otherwise retrieve the property ('idha lam yaqdir 'ala akhudh minhu').
This limits the ruling; if retrieval is possible through other means, killing is unlawful.
5
Note the Ikhtilaf
The Hanafi school allows lethal force for property alone, but other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) restrict it to cases where the thief poses a threat to life.
The practical difference is significant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن دخل عليه غيره، فأخرج السرقة، فأتبعه، فقتله، فلا شيء عليه. فلا شيء عليه لقوله : 'قاتل' دون مالك، أي لأجل مالك، ولأن له أن يمنعه بالقتل ابتداء، فكذا له أن يسترده به انتهاء إذا لم يقدر على أخذه منه
And if another enters upon him, and takes out the stolen property, and he follows him and kills him, then there is nothing upon him. 'There is nothing upon him' because of his saying: 'The killer' does not own, i.e., for the sake of the owner, and because he may prevent him by killing initially, so likewise he may retrieve it by killing at the end if he cannot take it from him.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage deals with a homeowner who kills a thief after the thief has taken property from the house and is fleeing. The ruling is that the homeowner is not liable for killing the thief. The proof is a hadith (possibly 'man qutila duna malihi fahuwa shahid' or similar) indicating that one who kills in defense of property is not liable. The commentary explains that just as the owner may use lethal force initially to prevent theft, he may also use it to recover the stolen property if he cannot otherwise retrieve it. This is based on the principle that the thief's life is forfeit due to his aggression.

Key Terms
دون مالك (duna malik): In defense of one's property; for the sake of the owner's right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary basis for the ruling that there is no liability for killing a thief who has taken property?
A) A Quranic verse on retaliation
B) A hadith about fighting in defense of property
C) Consensus of the Companions
D) Rational analogy to self-defense against bodily harm

Key Takeaway
A homeowner may lawfully kill a fleeing thief to recover property only if he cannot retrieve it by any other means.

Killing a Thief When Shouting Would Suffice
Ruling with qiyas proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on killing a thief when a non-lethal alternative is available and apply the qiyas from the usurpation case.

Core Idea
If an owner can retrieve property without killing, killing the thief incurs qisas—necessity does not justify lethal force.

In this passage, the Hanafi school clarifies the limits of defending property. While a determined thief may be killed if necessary, the moment a less harmful means (like shouting) suffices, the owner's right to kill is forfeited. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof by analogy (qiyas) to usurpation, and the exception for thieves who cannot be deterred by shouting.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and its condition
Identify the act: owner kills a thief when shouting would cause the thief to drop the property. The ruling is qisas (retaliation) wajib.
The condition 'if shouting would make him drop' is key.
2
Understand the proof: qiyas to usurpation
The text compares to a case of usurpation (ghasb): if the victim kills the usurper when he could seek help from Muslims and the judge, qisas is due. The common illa is the availability of a less harmful means to achieve the objective.
This is qiyas jaliy (clear analogy).
3
Contrast with the determined thief
The exception: if the thief is not repelled by shouting (i.e., determined to keep the property despite risk of being caught), killing may be permitted. This is because no non-lethal alternative suffices.
The text says 'unlike the thief who is not repelled by shouting'.
4
Apply the principle of proportionality
The Hanafi rule: deadly force is only justified when necessary to protect life or property, and only when no lesser means can achieve the same end.
This principle governs many fiqh rulings on self-defense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أنه لو صاح عليه يطرح ماله، فقتله ذلك، تجب عليه القصاص. تجب عليه القصاص؛ لأن قتله بغير حق، وهو بمنزلة المغصوب منه إذا قتل الغاصب حيث يجب عليه القصاص؛ لأنه يقدر على دفعه بالاستعانة بالمسلمين والقاضي، فلا تسقط عصمته بخلاف السارق الذي لا يندفع بالصياح. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٨١/٩]
And if, if he were to shout at him, he would drop his property, and he kills him, then qisas is obligatory upon him. 'Qisas is obligatory upon him' because he killed him without right, and he is like one whose property is wrongfully taken if he kills the usurper, where qisas is obligatory upon him, because he can repel him by seeking help from Muslims and the judge, so his inviolability is not forfeited, unlike the thief who is not repelled by shouting. Completion of al-Bahr al-Raiq: 81/9.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage contrasts with the previous one. If the owner can retrieve his property by merely shouting (implying the thief would drop it and flee), but instead kills the thief, then qisas is due. The reason is that the owner has a less harmful alternative (shouting for help or to scare the thief). Since he could achieve his goal without killing, the killing is unjust. The commentary analogizes this to a case of usurpation (ghasb): if a person whose property is wrongfully taken kills the usurper when he could seek help from authorities, he is subject to qisas. However, if the thief is not deterred by shouting (e.g., he is determined), then killing may be permitted. This establishes a principle of proportionality in the Hanafi law of self-defense and property protection.

Key Terms
يطرح ماله (yatruhu malahu): To drop or abandon the stolen property (out of fear).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a property owner kills a thief when shouting would have made the thief drop the stolen property?
A) No liability, because theft is a crime
B) Qisas is obligatory
C) Diyah only
D) The owner is rewarded

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school upholds the sanctity of life: lethal force against a thief is only justified when no less harmful means can retrieve the property.

Diyah for Killing a Madman or Child Attacker
Ruling on diyah for killing a child or insane attacker combined with an inter-madhhab rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diyah for killing a child or insane person who attacks, and explain how the Hanafi school rebuts the Shafi'i objection.

Core Idea
Killing a child or madman attacker incurs diyah because their act does not remove inviolability.

This passage addresses a critical question: if a child or an insane person draws a weapon and attacks, and you kill them in self-defense, are you liable? The Hanafi school distinguishes between the permissibility of the defensive act and the continued inviolability of the attacker. Imam al-Shafi'i argues that self-defense absolves all liability, but the Hanafis maintain that diyah is wajib on the killer's property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is killing a child or insane person who is attacking. The ruling: diyah is wajib on the killer's property.
2
Understand the Shafi'i objection
Al-Shafi'i holds no liability because the killing was in self-defense. Identify his dalil: the killer acted as a defender (dāfi').
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi response: the act of the child/insane is not characterized as transgression (baghy), so their inviolability (isma) is not lost. Qisas would logically follow but is not applied because there is a valid excuse (repelling harm). Instead, diyah is due.
Focus on the concept of 'isma and the distinction between the permissibility of the act and the liability for damages.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis require diyah payment; Shafi'is absolve the killer entirely.
This is the real-world consequence of the ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تجب الدية، وعلى هذا الصبي والدابة ولو ضربه الشاهر فانصرف، فقتله الآخر، أي في ماله الشاهر بعد الضرب الشاهر قتل القاتل. ومن دخل عليه غيره، فأخرج السرقة، فأتبعه، فقتله، فلا شيء عليه. وهو المشهور عليه المال المسروق صاحب البيت رب البيت السارق عمدا عن القاتل نفسه، تجب الدية أي في ماله؛ لأن العواقل لا تعقل العمد وقال الشافعي: لا شيء عليه؛ لأنه قتله دافعا ولنا: أن فعل المجنون والصبي والدابة لا يوصف بالخطر، فلم يقع بغيا، فلا تسقط العصمة، ومقتضاه القصاص، إلا أنه لا يجب لوجود المبيح، وهو دفع الشر. (فتح) وعلى هذا الصبي إلخ [ أي على الحكم المذكور في المجنون والصبي والدابة يعني إذا شهر الصبي على غيره سلاحا، فقتله المشهور عليه عمدا تجب عليه الدية، خلافا للشافعي؛ لعدم الاختيار الصحيح منه، وكذا الدابة إذا صالت على إنسان فقتلها يجب عليه الضمان عندنا خلافا للشافعي هو يقول: إنه قتلها دافعا عن نفسه، وكونها مملوكة للغير لا تأثير له في وجوب الضمان كالعبد إذا شهر سيفا على رجل، فقتله، فإنه لا يجب عليه الضمان، فكذا هذا، فصار كالصيد إذا صال على المحرم، فقتله، ونحن نقول: إن فعل الدابة غير معتبر أصلا، حتى لا يعتبر في وجوب الضمان؛ لأن العجماء جبار، وعصمتها لحق مالكها. بخلاف عصمة عبد الغير، فإنها لحق نفسه، وفعله محظور، فتسقط به عصمته وبخلاف الصيد إذا صال على المحرم؛ لأن الشارع أذن في قتله، ولم يوجب علينا تحمل أذاه ألا ترى أن الخمس الفواسق أباح قتلها مطلقا؛ لتوهم الأذى منها، فما ظنك عند تحقق الأذى، وعن أبي يوسف أنه يجب الضمان في الدابة ولا يجب في الصبي والمجنون؛ لأن فعل المجنون والصبي معتبر في الجملة، ولهذا يجب عليهما ضمان ما أتلفاه وفعل الدابة غير معتبر أصلا؛ ولأن عصمتهما لحقهما، وعصمة الدابة لحق المالك من العيني والفتح وملا مسكين بتغيير وتصرف)
The diyah is obligatory, and upon this (ruling is) the child and the animal. If the one who drew a weapon [شاهر] struck and then turned away, and the other (the victim) killed him, the diyah is on the property of the striker after the strike. The killer himself: diyah is due on his property because the 'aqlas (kinship groups) do not bear responsibility for intentional killing. Al-Shafi'i said: nothing is due on him because he killed (him) as a defender. Our argument: The act of the insane person, child, and animal is not described as dangerous (for criminal liability), so it did not occur as transgression, thus inviolability is not dropped. Its consequence is qisas, except it is not obligatory due to the presence of that which permits it, namely repelling harm. (Fath) And upon this (ruling is) the child etc. [i.e., concerning the ruling mentioned for the insane, child, and animal, meaning if a child draws a weapon against another and the one at whom it is drawn kills him intentionally, diyah is obligatory on him, contrary to al-Shafi'i, due to the lack of proper choice on his part. Similarly, if an animal attacks a person and he kills it, the guarantee is obligatory according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i. He says: he killed it in self-defense, and its being owned by another has no effect on the obligation of guarantee, like a slave who draws a sword against a man and then is killed – no guarantee is obligatory. So too this case. It becomes like game that attacks a pilgrim and he kills it. And we say: the act of an animal is not considered at all, so it is not considered for obligation of guarantee, because 'an animal is a jubbār (injury by it is free of liability),' and its inviolability is for the right of its owner. Unlike the inviolability of another's slave, which is for the sake of the slave himself, and his act is prohibited and thus his inviolability is dropped. And unlike game that attacks a pilgrim, because the Lawgiver has permitted killing it and has not obligated us to bear its harm. Do you not see that the five harmful (animals) are permitted to be killed absolutely due to the possibility of harm? What then when harm is actualized? And from Abu Yusuf: guarantee is obligatory for the animal but not for the child and insane person, because the act of the insane and child is considered in a general sense, hence they are liable for what they damage, while the act of the animal is not considered at all; and because their inviolability (child/insane) is for their own right, while the inviolability of the animal is for the right of its owner. From al-'Ayni, al-Fath, and Mulla Miskin with modification and arrangement).]
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute and a ruling with proof regarding the liability for killing a person who lacks full legal capacity (child, insane person) or an animal when they are aggressors. The text begins by stating that diyah is obligatory on the killer's property, including in the case of a madman, child, or animal. It then narrates a specific scenario: if a person draws a weapon and strikes, then turns away, and the victim kills that striker, diyah is due from the striker's property. This is the Hanafi position. The commentary explains that the same ruling applies to children and animals. The text then engages in dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that nothing is due because the killing was in self-defense. The Hanafi response argues that the act of an insane person, child, or animal is not considered a transgression (baghy) in a way that would remove inviolability (isma). While qisas would be the logical consequence, it is not applied because there is a valid excuse (repelling harm). However, diyah remains. The commentary expands on this, noting that a child's act lacks proper choice, so diyah takes the place of qisas. For animals, the Hanafis argue that their actions are not legally recognized at all, relying on the hadith 'al-'Ajma'u jabbār (injury caused by an animal is free of liability). This is in contrast to the case of a slave, whose actions are considered and can remove his own inviolability. The passage also includes an intra-Hanafi opinion from Imam Abu Yusuf, who distinguishes between animals (liability) and children/insane (no liability), but the relied-upon position (mufta bih) is the one stating diyah is obligatory in all these cases. The text also references the rule of five harmful animals (al-fawāsiq) which are permitted to be killed even in ihram, by analogy.

Key Terms
عصمة (‘iṣma): Legal inviolability of a person; for humans, it is inherent and cannot be removed without a legally recognized transgression (baghy).
بغي (baghy): Transgression or rebellious aggression that removes the inviolability of the aggressor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if you kill a child who is attacking you, what is the financial obligation?
A) No financial obligation because the child was the aggressor.
B) Diyah wajib on your property.
C) Qisas because killing a child is murder.
D) Compensation is on the child's estate.

Key Takeaway
Killing a child or insane person in self-defense requires diyah on the killer's property because their inviolability is not extinguished by their act.

Guarantee for Killing an Attacking Animal
Ruling on guarantee for killing an attacking animal, including inter-madhhab and intra-Hanafi disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on guaranteeing an animal killed in self-defense, distinguish it from the Shafi'i and Abu Yusuf positions, and explain the role of the hadith 'al-'Ajma'u jabbār' and the concept of 'isma for animals.

Core Idea
Killing an attacking animal requires compensation because its inviolability belongs to its owner.

This passage continues the discussion of diyah for killing an attacker, focusing on the case of an animal. While the Hanafi school holds that diyah is due for killing any attacker who lacks capacity, there is a nuance for animals: the animal's act is not legally considered at all, and its inviolability is solely for the owner's right. This leads to a divergence: Abu Yusuf restricts liability to the animal only, while the majority extends it to humans as well. The text also engages with Shafi'i, who analogizes to a slave or game in ihram.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for animals
The Hanafi ruling: if an animal attacks and you kill it, you must pay compensation (damān) to the owner.
This is the same as diyah for humans but technically damān for property.
2
Understand the Shafi'i analogy
Al-Shafi'i analogizes the animal to a slave or game: if a slave draws a weapon and is killed, no guarantee; if game attacks a pilgrim, killing is permitted without compensation. So he argues no guarantee here.
He relies on the principle of self-defense overriding liability.
3
Analyze the Hanafi distinction
The Hanafi response: an animal's act is not legally recognized at all (ghayr mu'tabar), unlike a slave's act which is considered. The hadith 'al-'Ajma'u jabbār' shows that no liability attaches for the animal's own damage, but killing the animal is different: its inviolability is for the owner's right, so compensation is due.
Note the distinction: the animal's act does not remove its inviolability because it lacks legal capacity, but the slave's act can remove his own inviolability.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf holds that guarantee is only for the animal, not for the child or insane person, because the animal's act is not considered at all, while the acts of humans are considered in a general sense.
This is a minority opinion; the mufta bihi is the general position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تجب الدية، وعلى هذا الصبي والدابة ولو ضربه الشاهر فانصرف، فقتله الآخر، أي في ماله الشاهر بعد الضرب الشاهر قتل القاتل. ومن دخل عليه غيره، فأخرج السرقة، فأتبعه، فقتله، فلا شيء عليه. وهو المشهور عليه المال المسروق صاحب البيت رب البيت السارق عمدا عن القاتل نفسه، تجب الدية أي في ماله؛ لأن العواقل لا تعقل العمد وقال الشافعي: لا شيء عليه؛ لأنه قتله دافعا ولنا: أن فعل المجنون والصبي والدابة لا يوصف بالخطر، فلم يقع بغيا، فلا تسقط العصمة، ومقتضاه القصاص، إلا أنه لا يجب لوجود المبيح، وهو دفع الشر. (فتح) وعلى هذا الصبي إلخ [ أي على الحكم المذكور في المجنون والصبي والدابة يعني إذا شهر الصبي على غيره سلاحا، فقتله المشهور عليه عمدا تجب عليه الدية، خلافا للشافعي؛ لعدم الاختيار الصحيح منه، وكذا الدابة إذا صالت على إنسان فقتلها يجب عليه الضمان عندنا خلافا للشافعي هو يقول: إنه قتلها دافعا عن نفسه، وكونها مملوكة للغير لا تأثير له في وجوب الضمان كالعبد إذا شهر سيفا على رجل، فقتله، فإنه لا يجب عليه الضمان، فكذا هذا، فصار كالصيد إذا صال على المحرم، فقتله، ونحن نقول: إن فعل الدابة غير معتبر أصلا، حتى لا يعتبر في وجوب الضمان؛ لأن العجماء جبار، وعصمتها لحق مالكها. بخلاف عصمة عبد الغير، فإنها لحق نفسه، وفعله محظور، فتسقط به عصمته وبخلاف الصيد إذا صال على المحرم؛ لأن الشارع أذن في قتله، ولم يوجب علينا تحمل أذاه ألا ترى أن الخمس الفواسق أباح قتلها مطلقا؛ لتوهم الأذى منها، فما ظنك عند تحقق الأذى، وعن أبي يوسف أنه يجب الضمان في الدابة ولا يجب في الصبي والمجنون؛ لأن فعل المجنون والصبي معتبر في الجملة، ولهذا يجب عليهما ضمان ما أتلفاه وفعل الدابة غير معتبر أصلا؛ ولأن عصمتهما لحقهما، وعصمة الدابة لحق المالك من العيني والفتح وملا مسكين بتغيير وتصرف)
The diyah is obligatory, and upon this (ruling is) the child and the animal. If the one who drew a weapon [شاهر] struck and then turned away, and the other (the victim) killed him, the diyah is on the property of the striker after the strike. The killer himself: diyah is due on his property because the 'aqlas (kinship groups) do not bear responsibility for intentional killing. Al-Shafi'i said: nothing is due on him because he killed (him) as a defender. Our argument: The act of the insane person, child, and animal is not described as dangerous (for criminal liability), so it did not occur as transgression, thus inviolability is not dropped. Its consequence is qisas, except it is not obligatory due to the presence of that which permits it, namely repelling harm. (Fath) And upon this (ruling is) the child etc. [i.e., concerning the ruling mentioned for the insane, child, and animal, meaning if a child draws a weapon against another and the one at whom it is drawn kills him intentionally, diyah is obligatory on him, contrary to al-Shafi'i, due to the lack of proper choice on his part. Similarly, if an animal attacks a person and he kills it, the guarantee is obligatory according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i. He says: he killed it in self-defense, and its being owned by another has no effect on the obligation of guarantee, like a slave who draws a sword against a man and then is killed – no guarantee is obligatory. So too this case. It becomes like game that attacks a pilgrim and he kills it. And we say: the act of an animal is not considered at all, so it is not considered for obligation of guarantee, because 'an animal is a jubbār (injury by it is free of liability),' and its inviolability is for the right of its owner. Unlike the inviolability of another's slave, which is for the sake of the slave himself, and his act is prohibited and thus his inviolability is dropped. And unlike game that attacks a pilgrim, because the Lawgiver has permitted killing it and has not obligated us to bear its harm. Do you not see that the five harmful (animals) are permitted to be killed absolutely due to the possibility of harm? What then when harm is actualized? And from Abu Yusuf: guarantee is obligatory for the animal but not for the child and insane person, because the act of the insane and child is considered in a general sense, hence they are liable for what they damage, while the act of the animal is not considered at all; and because their inviolability (child/insane) is for their own right, while the inviolability of the animal is for the right of its owner. From al-'Ayni, al-Fath, and Mulla Miskin with modification and arrangement).]
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute and a ruling with proof regarding the liability for killing a person who lacks full legal capacity (child, insane person) or an animal when they are aggressors. The text begins by stating that diyah is obligatory on the killer's property, including in the case of a madman, child, or animal. It then narrates a specific scenario: if a person draws a weapon and strikes, then turns away, and the victim kills that striker, diyah is due from the striker's property. This is the Hanafi position. The commentary explains that the same ruling applies to children and animals. The text then engages in dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that nothing is due because the killing was in self-defense. The Hanafi response argues that the act of an insane person, child, or animal is not considered a transgression (baghy) in a way that would remove inviolability (isma). While qisas would be the logical consequence, it is not applied because there is a valid excuse (repelling harm). However, diyah remains. The commentary expands on this, noting that a child's act lacks proper choice, so diyah takes the place of qisas. For animals, the Hanafis argue that their actions are not legally recognized at all, relying on the hadith 'al-'Ajma'u jabbār (injury caused by an animal is free of liability). This is in contrast to the case of a slave, whose actions are considered and can remove his own inviolability. The passage also includes an intra-Hanafi opinion from Imam Abu Yusuf, who distinguishes between animals (liability) and children/insane (no liability), but the relied-upon position (mufta bih) is the one stating diyah is obligatory in all these cases. The text also references the rule of five harmful animals (al-fawāsiq) which are permitted to be killed even in ihram, by analogy.

Key Terms
ضمان (damān): Compensation or guarantee for property loss; in this context, the value of the animal that was killed.
فواسق (fawāsiq): The five harmful animals (crow, kite, scorpion, mouse, wild dog/beast) whose killing is permitted even in ihram due to their harmful nature.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (majority), if a person kills an attacking owned animal, what is the financial obligation?
A) No obligation because of the hadith 'al-'ajma'u jabbār'.
B) Compensation (damān) wajib on the killer to the owner.
C) Diyah for the animal.
D) Only compensation if the animal was not one of the five harmful animals.

Key Takeaway
Killing an attacking owned animal requires compensation to its owner because the animal's act is not legally recognized and its inviolability belongs to the owner.

Liability for Killing a Thief: When Is It Permissible?
Ruling on killing a thief when recovering property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on killing a thief in hot pursuit and identify the condition that determines liability.

Core Idea
Killing a thief is permissible only when shouting cannot stop him; otherwise, it is murder.

The sanctity of life is paramount in Islamic law, but so is the right to protect property. This lesson explores a nuanced Hanafi position: the owner who kills a thief while recovering stolen goods may be exempt from liability, but only if no lesser means—like shouting—would have worked. We will dissect the reasoning and its limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is killing a thief while pursuing him to recover stolen property. The default ruling? The text begins: 'So nothing is due on him' — meaning no civil or criminal liability under certain conditions.
The matn phrase 'فلا شيء عليه' sets the stage.
2
Find the key condition
The liability depends on whether the thief could have been stopped without killing. Specifically: if the owner knows that shouting would make the thief drop the property, then killing becomes murder and qisas is wajib.
The condition is 'لو علم أنه لو صاح عليه يطرح ماله'.
3
Analyze the proof (qiyas)
The text draws an analogy to the case of a usurper (ghasib). If the true owner kills the usurper despite being able to get help from authorities, qisas is due. Similarly, if the thief can be repelled by shouting, killing is unjust.
The illah (effective cause) is the availability of a non-lethal means of recovery.
4
Extract the two scenarios and rulings
Scenario 1: Owner cannot recover property except by killing → no liability. Scenario 2: Owner knows shouting would work, but kills anyway → qisas wajib.
The text explicitly contrasts these two cases.
5
Consider cross-madhhab difference
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may not permit killing for property at all or require stricter conditions. This reinforces the Hanafi position as a specific legal reasoning exercise.
The tradition_note in the Mukhtalaf Fih block acknowledges legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا شيء عليه لقوله : "قاتل" دون مالك، أي لأجل مالك، ولأن له أن يمنعه بالقتل ابتداء، فكذا له أن يسترده به انتهاء إذا لم يقدر على أخذه منه، ولو علم أنه لو صاح عليه يطرح ماله، فقتله ذلك، تجب عليه القصاص؛ لأن قتله بغير حق، وهو بمنزلة المغصوب منه إذا قتل الغاصب حيث يجب عليه القصاص؛ لأنه يقدر على دفعه بالاستعانة بالمسلمين والقاضي، فلا تسقط عصمته بخلاف السارق الذي لا يندفع بالصياح.
So nothing is due on him because of his saying: 'he fights' ['قاتل'] not 'owner' [مالك], i.e., for the sake of the owner. And because he may prevent him (the thief) by killing initially, so likewise he may retrieve it (the property) by killing at the end if he is unable to take it from him. And if he knows that if he shouts at him he would drop his property, yet he kills him, then qisas is obligatory upon him because he killed him without right. And this is comparable to the case of the person from whom property is wrongfully seized (al-maghṣūb minhu) if he kills the usurper (al-ghāṣib) – here qisas is also obligatory because he can repel him by seeking help from Muslims and the judge; thus his (the usurper's) inviolability is not lost. Unlike the thief who cannot be repelled by shouting.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling regarding the liability of a property owner who kills a thief while pursuing him to recover stolen goods. The text states that if the owner kills the thief and cannot otherwise take back his property, there is no liability on the owner. The reasoning: the thief is a 'fighter' (qātil) in the sense that he is committing aggression by taking property, and the owner has the right to prevent aggression even by killing if necessary. This right extends to recovering the property after the theft. However, if the owner knows that he could cause the thief to drop the stolen goods simply by shouting, and he kills him instead, then qisas is obligatory. The analogy: this is like the case of a person from whom property is usurped (ghaṣb); if the owner kills the usurper, qisas is due because the owner could seek help from authorities and thus the usurper's life is not forfeit. The thief who would respond to shouting is similar: his death is not needed, so killing him is unjust. Thus, the ruling distinguishes between a thief who can be repelled by shouting (no license to kill) and one who cannot (permission to kill if necessary to recover property). This is a nuanced Hanafi position balancing the right to property against the sanctity of life.

Key Terms
قاتل دون مالك (qātil dūna mālik): A fighter for the sake of the owner, not the owner himself. In the text, it means the thief is considered an aggressor (fighting) against the owner's property, and the owner acts to defend his property.
القصاص (al-qiṣāṣ): Retaliatory punishment: life for life in the case of intentional murder.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under Hanafi law, if a thief steals your property and you chase him, when does killing the thief become unlawful and incur qisas?
A) If you can retrieve the property by shouting and the thief would drop it.
B) If the thief is unarmed.
C) If the value of the stolen property is less than one dinar.
D) If you have witnesses.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on killing a thief balances property defense with sanctity of life: lethal force is only justified when no lesser means (like shouting) can recover the property.

Settlement for Intentional Homicide (Sulh)
States rules for settling intentional homicide with payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on payment in sulh for intentional homicide and apply it to scenarios involving multiple heirs or joint killers.

Core Idea
Sulh for intentional homicide imposes immediate payment—any amount—because it is a contract.

Can the family of a murder victim agree to accept money instead of demanding execution? Islamic law permits this through a contract called sulh. In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi position that the payment is due immediately and is not capped by diyah, drawing on Quranic proof and comparing with accidental killing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic ruling
The text states that if the heirs settle with the killer for money, the payment becomes due immediately, whether little or much. Recognize that this is a contractual obligation (dyn) akin to a sale price.
This applies to intentional killing only.
2
Examine the scriptural proof
The text cites Quran 2:178: 'But whoever is granted a remission by his brother…' Ibn Abbas explains this verse was revealed concerning sulh. Note that the verse does not fix an amount, so the parties may agree on any sum.
The dalil is ahad (famous) but accepted as proof.
3
Distinguish intentional from accidental homicide
For accidental killing, settlement cannot exceed the diyah if the compensation is of the same type as diyah. Intentional killing has no such cap because the right to qisas can be exchanged for any amount by mutual consent.
This distinction is crucial for fatwa.
4
Analyze the case of joint killers
If a free man and a slave jointly kill, and an agent settles for both, the payment is divided equally between them because qisas is equally upon both. The text uses the analogy of a contract attributed to both.
The example shows how liability is apportioned.
5
Understand partial settlement by heirs
If only one heir (e.g., the wife) settles or forgives, the right to qisas of all heirs drops, and the remaining heirs' shares convert to diyah proportionally. Each heir has independent power over his share.
This prevents piecemeal execution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: أي هذا فصل في الصلح وغيره [ رمز الحقائق ٤٣٠/٢] ولما كان تصور الصلح بعد تصور الجناية و موجبها أتبع ذلك في فصل على حدة. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٩٤/٩] وجب حالا إلخ: أي إذا صالح أولياء المقتول القاتل على مال، وجب المال حالا، قليلا كان أو كثيرا؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَنْ عُفِيَ لَهُ مِنْ أَخِيهِ شَيْءٌ (البقرة: ۱۷۸ الآية قال ابن عباس لما نزلت في الصلح، ولما لم يكن فيه شيء مقدر فوض إلى اصطلاحهما كالخلع والكتابة والإعتاق على مال بخلاف ما إذا كان القتل خطأ، حيث لا يجوز بأكثر من الدية إذا كان الصلح على جنس ما افترضت فيه الدية وإن كان على خلاف الجنس يجوز وإن زاد على قدر الدية، وإنما وجب حالا؛ لأنه . دين وجب بالعقد، والأصل في مثله الحلول كالثمن والمهر، بخلاف الدية؛ لأنها لم تجب بالعقد، والحاصل: أن الحقوق التي يجوز إسقاطها بعوض لا يتقدر العوض فيها، وأصله الرد بالعيب إذا أسقط على مال إلخ. اعلم أنه ليس المراد من قول المصنف: "وجب المال "حالا عدم جواز تأجيله بدليل التنظير بالثمن والمهر، فالحاصل أنه لا يتأجل إلا بالشرط. (فتح وغيره) وينصف إلخ: معناه لو كان القاتل قتل حرا ،وعبدا، فأمر الحر القاتل ومولى العبد رجلا بأن يصالح عن دمهما على ألف درهم، ففعل المأمور، فالألف على الحر والعبد نصفان؛ لأنه مقابل بالقصاص، وهو عليهما على السواء، فيقسم بدله عليهما على السواء؛ ولأن الألف وجبت بالعقد، وهو مضاف إليهما، فينصف موجبه، وهو عليهما تكملة البحر الرائق: ٩٥/٩] عن :دمهما أي الدم العمد الذي اشتركا فيه. فلمن بقي حظه إلخ : صورة المسألة: أن رجلا قتل رجلا، وورثة المقتول زوجته وبنوه مثلا، فصالحت الزوجة عن حظها من القصاص، وهو الثمن على عوض، أو عفت سقط القصاص، وانقلب نصيب البنين مالا . من الدية، فيكون لهم سبعة ألمانها، وفي عكسه يعني إذا صالح البنون، أو عفوا، يكون للزوجة من الدية لمنها؛ لأن كل واحد يتمكن من التصرف في نصيبه استيفاء وإسقاطا بالعفو أو بالصلح، فإذا صالح أحدهم، أو عفا، سقط حق الباقين من القود؛ -
Chapter: This is a chapter on settlement and other matters. Since the concept of settlement comes after the concept of injury and its consequences, it is placed in a separate chapter. 'It becomes due immediately etc.': meaning if the heirs of the murdered person settle with the murderer for a sum of money, the money becomes due immediately, whether little or much, due to the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'But whoever is granted a remission by his brother...' (al-Baqarah 2:178). Ibn Abbas said it was revealed concerning settlement. Since there is no fixed amount, it is left to their agreement, like khul', kitabah, and emancipation for money. Unlike accidental killing, where settlement cannot exceed the diyah if it is on the same type of property as the diyah; if on a different type, it is permissible even if it exceeds the diyah. It is due immediately because it is a debt arising from a contract, and the principle for such is immediacy, like the price and the dower. Unlike diyah because it does not arise from contract. The gist: rights that can be dropped for compensation are not quantified in compensation, and its origin is the return for defect if dropped for money. Note: The author's statement 'the money is due immediately' does not mean it cannot be deferred, as shown by the analogy to price and dower; it is only deferred by condition. (Fath and others) 'And it is divided equally' meaning: if the killer killed a free man and a slave, and the free killer and the slave's master ordered a man to settle for their blood on a thousand dirhams, and the agent did so, the thousand is half on the free man and half on the slave because it is in exchange for qisas, which is equally upon them both, so its substitute is divided equally; also because the thousand became due by contract, which is attributed to both, so its obligation is divided equally upon them. (Supplement: 'their blood' meaning the intentional blood they jointly participated in.) 'So for whoever remains his share etc.' Image of the issue: A man killed a man, and the heirs are his wife and sons for example. The wife settled for her share of qisas, which is one-eighth, for compensation or forgave, the qisas drops, and the sons' share turns into money from diyah, so they have seven-eighths of it. And in the opposite case, if the sons settled or forgave, the wife gets her one-eighth of diyah. Because each one is able to dispose of his share, execution and dropping by forgiveness or settlement. So if one of them settles or forgives, the right of the remaining to qisas drops.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage covers the rules of sulh (settlement) for intentional homicide. The key points: 1) If heirs settle with the murderer for a payment, the payment is due immediately; it can be any amount because it's a contract-based debt, analogous to sale or dower. Proof: Quran 2:178. 2) If two offenders (a free man and a slave) authorize someone to settle for their blood, the payment is divided equally between them because qisas is equally upon both. 3) If only one heir settles or forgives, the right to qisas of the remaining heirs falls, and their shares convert to diyah proportionally. This shows each heir has independent power over his share of qisas.

Key Terms
الصلح عن دم العمد (al-ṣulḥ ʿan dam al-ʿamd): A contractual settlement where the heirs of a murder victim agree to drop qisas in exchange for a sum of money.
القود (al-qawd): qisas, specifically retaliatory execution for murder.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on the payment in a settlement for intentional homicide?
A) Must be exactly equal to diyah.
B) Can be any amount and is due immediately.
C) Must be deferred.
D) Not allowed.

Key Takeaway
Sulh for intentional homicide creates an immediate payment obligation with no fixed amount, because it is a contract based on mutual agreement, unlike diyah in accidental killing.

One Diya for Multiple Inadvertent Injuries Without Healing
Ruling on multiple inadvertent injuries with non-healing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on multiple inadvertent injuries without intervening healing, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute, and apply the principle that diya is not an intelligible equivalent.

Core Idea
Two continuous inadvertent injuries without healing are treated as one act for diya — only one diya is due.

In Hanafi law, when multiple inadvertent injuries occur without the victim recovering between them, they are considered a single continuous act. This lesson explores the ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, the rational proof (istihsan) behind it, and the differing views of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exception
The general rule is that each injury is considered separately unless they are two inadvertent injuries with no healing between them.
The exception is stated in the text: 'except in two inadvertent injuries with no healing between them'.
2
State the Hanafi position
Abu Hanifa holds that only one diya is due for the combined act that leads to death.
This is the mufta bihi position according to al-Marghinani.
3
Understand the proof
Diya is a non-intelligible equivalent (mithlun ma'qul), so the default is non-obligation unless the effect clearly continues. The second injury completes the first, so only one diya is owed.
This contrasts with qisas, which is an intelligible equivalent.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf prescribes a discretionary assessment (hukuma) for healed injuries; Muhammad requires payment for medical costs in addition to diya for life.
The consensus is that if a trace remains after healing, its compensation is due along with diya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحكام تعدد الجناية
بينهما برء أو لا، إلا في خطئين لم يتخلل بينهما برء، فتجب دية واحدة، كمن ضربه مائة سوط، فبرئ من تسعين ومات من عشرة. ... إلا في خطئين إلخ استثناء من قوله: "أخذ بالأمرين، فلا يؤخذ فيه إلا بدية واحدة لا غير؛ لأن دية القطع إنما تجب عند استحكام أثر الفعل، وهو أن يعلم عدم السراية والفرق بين هذه الصورة وبين عمدين لا برء بينهما عنده أن الدية مثل غير معقول، فالأصل عدم وجوبها، بخلاف القصاص، فإنه مثل معقول. (عيني، فتح) كمن ضربه أي كما يجب دية واحدة في من ضربه مائة. فبرئ إلخ : فإنه يجب فيه دية واحدة؛ لأن الضربات التي برئ منها، ولم يبق لها أثره، سقط أرشها لزوال الشين، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة، وعند أبي يوسف: فيها حكومة محمد: أنه وعن يجب فيها أجرة الطبيب ومن الأدوية، ولو بقي لها أثر بعد البرئ يجب موجبه مع دية النفس بالإجماع؛ لأن الأرش يجب باعتبار الشين في النفس، وهو ببقاء الأثر. [رمز الحقائق: ٤٣٢/٢]
Rulings of multiple injuries: whether healing occurs between them or not, except in two inadvertent injuries with no healing between them, then one blood-money is obligatory. Like one who strikes him one hundred lashes, he recovers from ninety and dies from ten. ... The exception from his statement: 'Both are taken into account, so nothing is taken except one blood-money, no more; because the blood-money for amputation is only obligatory when the effect of the act is established, which is when non-extension is known, and the difference between this case and two intentional injuries with no healing between them according to him is that blood-money is a non-intelligible equivalent, so the default is non-obligation, unlike retaliation, which is an intelligible equivalent. (Ayni, Fath) Like one who strikes him: i.e., as one blood-money is obligatory in the case of one who strikes him one hundred. He recovers, etc.: So one blood-money is obligatory because the strikes from which he recovered, leaving no trace, their compensation is waived due to removal of the blemish, and this according to Abu Hanifa. According to Abu Yusuf: there is a discretionary assessment (hukuma). According to Muhammad: there must be payment for doctor and medicines. And if a trace remains after healing, its due must be paid along with the blood-money of the life by consensus, because compensation is owed on account of the blemish on the life, which is the persistence of the trace. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/432]
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage expounds the ruling on multiple injuries in Hanafi law, specifically when two inadvertent acts occur without any healing between them. The source text (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) states that in such a case only one diya (blood-money) is due. The commentator, al-Marghinani or the sharh, explains the reasoning: the diya for amputation (diyat al-qat') is not owed unless the effect of the injury is established, meaning it is known that the injury did not extend (sara) to the life. When there is no healing, the second injury is considered a completion of the first, so only one diya is due. This contrasts with two intentional injuries without healing, where according to Abu Hanifa they cannot be combined for fixed penalty (qisas), because qisas is an intelligible equivalent, while diya is not — the default is non-obligation. The passage then gives an example: a person who strikes another a hundred lashes, the victim recovers from ninety but dies from the effects of ten. Here only one diya is owed because the effects of the ninety lashes ceased, so their compensation drops. This view is specifically Abu Hanifa's. Abu Yusuf holds that a discretionary assessment (hukuma) is due for the ninety, while Muhammad requires payment for the doctor and medicines. The text adds that if a trace (shayn) remains after healing, the compensation for that trace is due alongside the diya for life by consensus, because the blemish persists.

Key Terms
دية (diya): Blood-money, a financial compensation for homicide or injury, replacing retaliation.
حكومة (hukuma): A discretionary compensation assessed by a judge for injuries that lack a fixed diya, based on the extent of damage.
شيْن (shayn): A blemish or disfigurement resulting from an injury, on which compensation (arsh) is based.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a person strikes another with 100 lashes, the victim recovers from 90 but dies from the remaining 10, what is due?
A) One diya for life only, no compensation for the 90 lashes.
B) Diya for life plus compensation for each lash.
C) Diya for life plus hukuma for the 90 lashes.
D) Diya for life plus doctor's fees.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, two inadvertent injuries without healing are treated as one for diya, based on the principle that diya is not an intelligible equivalent.
