Building in Public and Private Roads: Permissibility and Harm
Ruling on constructing structures in public vs private roads

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on constructing structures in public and private roads and apply the condition of harm to determine permissibility.

Core Idea
Public road use is free unless harm arises; private road use requires unanimous consent.

The classical Hanafi texts address everyday urban issues like building a latrine or projecting shelf onto a street. This lesson unpacks the principle that what harms the public is forbidden, while harmless use is permissible in public roads. In private roads, the ownership rights of residents require their collective permission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its location
Determine what is being built (latrine, waterspout, shop, etc.) and whether the road is a public thoroughfare (tariq nafidh) or a private cul-de-sac (ghayr nafidh).
The distinction is crucial for the ruling.
2
Apply the rule for public roads
In a public road, the basic ruling is permissibility (mubah) unless the structure causes harm (darar) to passersby. If harm occurs, it becomes prohibited and any eligible person may demand demolition.
The hadith 'La darara wa la dirara' is the key evidence.
3
Apply the rule for private roads
In a private road owned collectively by residents, no construction is allowed without the unanimous permission of all owners, regardless of harm.
Because it is common property, and obtaining consent is possible.
4
Consider intra-Hanafi differences and exceptions
Note that Imam Muhammad restricts the right of demolition to only those actually harmed, while the majority view allows any passerby to contest. Also, structures built for public benefit or with the imam's permission are not demolished.
The mufta bihi is the majority view as stated in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يحدث الرجل في الطريق من أخرج إلى طريق العامة كنيفا أو ميزابا أو جرصنا أو دكانا، فلكل نزعه، وله التصرف في النافذ إلا إذا أضر، وفي غيره لا يتصرف إلا بإذنهم، بالعامة أي في النافذ مطلقاً أضربهم أولا من أهل الخصومة ولو ذميا باب ما يحدث إلخ لما فرغ من بيان أحكام القتل مباشرة شرع في بيان أحكامه تسبيبا، وقدم الأول؛ لكونه أصلا؛ لأنه قتل بلا واسطة ولكونه أكثر وقوعا، فكان أمس حاجة إلى معرفة أحكامه. [تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٢٦/٩] أو جرصنا: [هو البرج قيل: محرى ماء يركب في الحائط وهو الممر على العلو، وهو مثل الرف، الخشبة الموضوعة على جدار السطحين؛ ليتمكن من المرور، وقيل: هو الذي يعمل قدام الطاقة لتوضع عليه كيزان ونحوها. رمز الحقائق: ٤٤٨/٢] وقيل: هي فلكل نزعه أي لكل واحد من أهل المرور والخصومة مطالبة بالنقض إذا كان ممن يملك التصرف، ولو بالاذن كالمسلم والبالغ العاقل والصبي والعبد المأذونين وكالذمي؛ لأن لكل منهم المرور بنفسه ودوابه، فتكون له الخصومة بنفسه كما في الملك المشترك، بخلاف العبيد والصبيان المحجور عليهم حيث لا يؤمر بالهدم بمطالبتهم؛ لأن مخاصمة المحجور عليهم لا تعتبر في ماله، هذا إذا بنى لنفسه بغير إذن الإمام؛ وإذا بنى للمسلمين كالمسجد ونحوه، أو بنى بإذن الإمام لا ينقض وعند محمد والثلاثة ليس لكل نزعه إذا لم يضربهم، وقال إسماعيل الصفار: إنما ينقض بخصومته إذا لم يكن له مثل ذلك، فإن كان له مثل ذلك لا يلتفت إلى خصومته. (عيني، فتح، تكملة) وله التصرف [ أي للرجل من أصحاب الأملاك] أي له أن يتصرف بإحداث الجرصن وغيره مما تقدم ذكره في الطريق النافذ إذا لم يضر بالعامة ولم يمنعه أحد، فإذا أضر بالعامة لا يحل؛ لقوله عليه السلام: "لا ضرر ولا ضرار في الإسلام" تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٦٣/٩] في النافذ الطريق النافذ بإحداث ما ذكر. وفي غيره إلخ : أي في غير النافذ من الطريق لا يتصرف أحد بإحداث ما ذكر إلا بإذن أهله؛ لأن الطريق التي ليست بنافذة مملوكة لأهلها، فهم فيه شركاء، ولهذا يستحقون بها الشفعة والمتصرف في الملك المشترك من الوجه الذي لم يوضع له لا يملك إلا بإذن الكل أضر بهم أو لم يضر، بخلاف النافذ؛ لأنه ليس لأحد فيه ملك، فيجوز الانتفاع به ما لم يضر بأحد، ولأنه إذا كان حق العامة فيتعذر الوصول إلى إذن الكل، فجعل كل واحد كأنه هو المالك وحده في حق الانتفاع ما لم يضر بأحد، ولا كذلك غير النافذ؛ لأن الوصول إلى إرضائهم ممكن، فيبقى على شركته حقيقة وحكما، وفي "المنتقى": إنما يؤمر برفع هذه الأشياء إذا علم حدوثها، فلو كانت قديمة، فليس لأحد حق الرفع، وإن لم يدر حال هذه الأشياء تجعل قديمة، وهذا هو الأصل. تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٦٤/٩] إلا ياذهم: بإذن أهل تلك الطريق.
Chapter on what a man constructs in the road. Whoever puts into a public road a latrine, or a waterspout, or a projecting wooden structure (jursun), or a shop, then everyone has the right to demolish it, and he has the right to dispose (build) in a thoroughfare unless it causes harm. In other (non-thoroughfare) roads, he may not dispose except with their permission. 'al-'amma' means in the thoroughfare absolutely, whether they are harmed or not. Among the people entitled to contest are even a dhimmi. The chapter on what a man constructs etc.: When he finished explaining the rulings of direct killing, he began explaining rulings of causation (tasbib), and he gave precedence to the first because it is primary and more frequent, so it was more needed. [Takmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 9/126] Or 'jursun': It is a tower, or a water channel built in the wall, or a high passage, like a shelf (raft) placed on the wall of two roofs to enable crossing, or it is what is made in front of a window to place jugs etc. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/448] And it is said: it is. Then 'everyone has the right to demolish it': meaning every passerby or contestant has the right to demand its removal if he is authorized to dispose, even by permission, such as a Muslim, a sane adult, a minor, a permitted slave, and a dhimmi; because each of them has the right to pass with himself and his animals, so he has the right to contest individually, as in common ownership. This is unlike forbidden slaves and minors who are interdicted, as their contestation is not considered in their property. This is if he built for himself without the imam's permission. If he built for the Muslims like a mosque, or with the imam's permission, it is not demolished. According to Muhammad and the three (schools), not everyone has the right to demolish it if they are not harmed. Isma'il al-Saffar said: It is only demolished by his contest if he does not have a similar one; if he has a similar one, his contest is not heeded. (al-'Ayni, al-Fath, Takmila) 'And he has the right to dispose': i.e., the man (owner) has the right to build a jursun etc. in a public thoroughfare if it does not harm the public and no one prevents him. If it harms the public, it is not permissible due to the hadith: 'No harm and no reciprocating harm in Islam' [Takmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 9/163]. 'In the thoroughfare': meaning the public road, by building what was mentioned. 'And in other than it' etc.: In a non-thoroughfare road, one may not build anything except with permission of its residents, because a road that is not a thoroughfare is owned by its residents, they are partners in it, and due to that they deserve pre-emption (shuf'a). The one who disposes in common property in a way not permitted cannot do so except with permission of all, whether harmful or not. This is unlike the thoroughfare because no one owns it, so one may benefit as long as no harm, and because it is a public right, obtaining everyone's permission is difficult, so each person is regarded as sole owner for benefit as long as no harm. This is not so for a non-thoroughfare because satisfying them is possible, so it remains common property in reality and in law. In al-Muntaqa: One is only ordered to remove these things if they are known to be newly built; if they are old, no one has the right to remove them. And if the status of these things is unknown, they are considered old. This is the principle. [Takmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 9/164] 'illa bi-idhnihim': except with permission of the residents of that road.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on what a man may construct in a road (bab ma yuhdith al-rajul fi al-tariq). The author begins by stating the general rule: if someone builds a latrine (kanif), a waterspout (mizab), a projecting structure (jursun), or a shop (dukkan) in a public road (tariq al-'amma), then every person has the right to demand its demolition (naz'). The builder has the right to construct in a through road (al-nafidh) as long as it does not cause harm (darar). In a non-through road (ghayr al-nafidh), he may not construct without the permission of the owners (ahl al-tariq). The commentary explains that 'al-'amma' refers to the thoroughfare absolutely, whether harm occurs or not. Those entitled to contest include any person who has the right to pass, even a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject). The text then provides the reason for the chapter ordering: having finished with direct killing, the author now discusses causation (tasbib). Definitions of 'jursun' are given: a tower, water channel, high passage, or shelf. The commentary by al-'Ayni, al-Fath, and Takmila explains that anyone with right of passage or contestation can demand removal if he is legally capable (mumayyiz, even a slave with permission). However, for a non-muslim (like a forbidden slave) or interdicted minor, their contest is not considered. If the builder built for himself without the imam's permission, it may be demolished; but if he built for the public benefit (like a mosque) or with the imam's permission, it is not demolished. There is an intra-Hanafi difference: according to Muhammad and the three (other schools), not everyone has the right to demolish if they are not harmed. Isma'il al-Saffar opined that demolition only occurs if the contestant does not have a similar structure themselves. The text then affirms the general rule: the builder may dispose in a thoroughfare as long as no harm, because of the hadith 'la darara wa la dirara fi al-Islam'. In a non-thoroughfare, because the road is owned by the residents (they are partners), permission of all is required irrespective of harm, unlike the thoroughfare which is public property. Finally, the text cites al-Muntaqa: only newly built structures can be removed; old ones remain, and if unknown, they are presumed old.

Key Terms
الطريق النافذ (al-tariq al-nafidh): A thoroughfare public road that leads to a public destination.
الكنيف (al-kanif): A latrine or privy built adjacent to a road.
الجرصن (al-jursun): A projecting wooden structure built out from a wall, like a shelf or a water channel.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, under what condition can a person build a structure in a public thoroughfare (tariq nafidh)?
A) Only with permission of the imam.
B) Only if no one objects.
C) As long as it does not cause harm to the public.
D) Only in private roads, not public.

Key Takeaway
Building in a public road is permissible unless it harms the public; building in a private road requires unanimous consent of all owners.

Liability for a Leaning Wall Built from the Start
States liability for a wall built leaning initially

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for a wall built leaning from the start and apply the distinction between minor and excessive leaning.

Core Idea
A leaning wall built from the start incurs liability for damages even without request.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a common property dispute: who is liable when a leaning wall collapses? The Hanafi school holds the builder responsible if the lean was excessive from the beginning, analogizing it to other encroachments. However, a minor, unavoidable lean does not trigger liability. We will unpack the ruling, its proof, and the important nuance that distinguishes minor from excessive leaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the specific act? Building a wall that is leaning from the time of construction. What is the ruling? The builder is liable for any damage caused by its fall, even if no one asked him to demolish it.
Liability here means financial compensation (damān) for property destroyed.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author provide? The author uses qiyas (analogy): the builder's act is comparable to projecting a wing, placing a stone, or digging a well in a public road—all considered encroachments (ta'addī).
The common illa (effective cause) is the unlawful encroachment on another's right.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How exactly does the analogy prove liability? In each case, the person performing the encroachment must ensure it does not cause harm. Building a leaning wall is an encroachment because it imposes a risk on neighbours or passersby.
The illa is the element of transgression (ta'addī) that necessitates safeguarding.
4
Note the ikhtilaf within the school
The author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq did not distinguish between minor and excessive leaning. However, al-Muntaqā (a later Hanafi text) makes this distinction: minor leaning is unavoidable and does not incur liability; excessive leaning does.
This intra-Hanafi refinement is crucial for the muftā bihi position.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Which position is relied upon for fatwa? The later scholars adopt al-Muntaqā's distinction. Minor leaning (yasīr) is excused because no wall is perfectly plumb; only a grossly excessive lean (fāḥish) triggers liability.
This shows how Hanafi fiqh balances strict liability with practical necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الحائط المائل
وإن بناه مائلا ابتداء ضمن ما تلف بسقوطه بلا طلب.
وإن بناه إلخ: يعني إن بنى الحائط حال كونه مائلا من الابتداء، ضمن رب الحائط ما تلف بسقوطه بلا طلب من أحد؛ لأنه تعدى بالبناء، فصار كإشراع الجناح ووضع الحجر وحفر البئر في الطريق، وأطلق المؤلف في الميلان، ولم يفرق بين يسيره وفاحشه. وفي "المنتقى": إن كان يسيرا وقت البناء لا يضمن؛ لأن الجدار لا يخلو عن يسير الميلان، وإن كان فاحشا يضمن، وإن لم يتقدم إليه أحد يطلب منه النقض.
Chapter on the leaning wall. If he builds it leaning from the start, he is liable for what is destroyed by its fall without request. "And if he builds it, etc." means: If he built the wall while it was leaning from the beginning, the owner of the wall is liable for what is destroyed by its fall without request from anyone, because he transgressed by building, so it becomes like projecting a wing, placing a stone, and digging a well in the road. The author was general in (mentioning) leaning, and did not distinguish between minor and excessive leaning. In al-Muntaqa: If it was minor at the time of building, he is not liable, because a wall is not free from minor leaning; but if it was excessive, he is liable, even if no one approached him to request demolition.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof. The passage opens by stating the ruling: if someone builds a wall leaning from the start, he is liable for any damage caused by its fall, even without anyone asking him to demolish it. The commentator explains that this is because the builder transgressed by building a dangerous structure, analogous to projecting a wing, placing a stone, or digging a well in a public road—all of which are encroachments. The author did not differentiate between minor and excessive leaning, but al-Muntaqā notes that minor leaning is unavoidable in walls and thus does not incur liability, whereas excessive leaning does.

Key Terms
إشراع الجناح (ishrā‘ al-janāḥ): Projecting a wing or extension of a building into public space.
الميلان (al-maylān): Leaning of a wall from the vertical; the text distinguishes between minor (yasīr) and excessive (fāḥish).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a wall is built with a slight (minor) lean from the start?
A) The owner is liable for any damage caused by its fall.
B) The owner is not liable because minor leaning is unavoidable.
C) The owner must request permission from authorities before building.
D) The owner is liable only if someone demanded the wall be demolished.

Key Takeaway
A builder is liable for a leaning wall's fall only if the lean is excessive from the start; minor leaning is excused as unavoidable.

Wall Leaning to Private House vs Public Road
Distinguishes request for demolition for wall leaning to private house vs public road

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the ruling on discharging or deferring demolition for a leaning wall on private property versus a public road, and apply the correct procedure based on who holds the right.

Core Idea
For private property, the owner alone can discharge; for public roads, only the judge may act.

When a wall leans precariously, who can the builder approach to avoid immediate demolition? The Hanafi school makes a sharp distinction: if the wall threatens a private house, the owner of that house holds the right and can personally discharge or delay the obligation. If the wall leans onto a public road, the right belongs to the entire Muslim community, and only the judge (qadi) can validly authorize relief. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies the ruling and its rational proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text contrasts a wall leaning to a private house (dar al-insan) with a wall leaning to a public road (tariq al-‘amm). Note the different right-holders.
Private: specific owner. Public: Muslim community.
2
Private house: determine who can act
For a private house, the request for demolition must be made to the owner (or tenant). Only that owner can grant discharge (ibra’) or deferment (ta’jil). If the wall falls after such relief, the builder is not liable.
The genitive 'house of a person' indicates minimal relation; the right is specific to the occupant.
3
Public road: explain the judge's role
For a public road, no individual can grant discharge or deferment. Only the judge can act, because the right belongs to all Muslims. The judge's action must be in their benefit, not harm.
Individual discharge is invalid except concerning one's own right (i.e., personal inconvenience).
4
Understand the principle behind the distinction
The key principle: the right-holder determines who may grant relief. Private rights are individual; public rights are collective and require a representative (the judge).
The judge acts as guardian of public interest.
5
Extend to other similar situations
The text clarifies that 'house of a man' is an example, not a restriction. The same ruling applies if an upper floor leans to a lower floor, or vice versa, in cases of separate ownership.
The principle is general: the right belongs to the affected party.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالطلب إلى ربها أي إلى رب الدار سواء كان مالكا أو ساكنا بإجارة أو غيرها، فالإضافة في قوله: "دار إنسان" لأدنى ملابسة ووجهه أن الحق لرب الدار على الخصوص، فيكون الطلب مفوضا إليه خاصة لا إلى غيره. (فتح وغيره) صح إلخ: أي صح إبراء رب الدار وتأجيله حتى لو سقط في صورة الإبراء أو قبل مضي المدة في صورة التأجيل، لا يضمن بخلاف ما إذا مال الحائط إلى الطريق العام، فأجله القاضي، ومن طلب النقض أو إبرائه لا يصح التأجيل والإبراء إلا في حق نفسه؛ لأن الحق لجماعة المسلمين، وتصرف القاضي في حق العامة نافذ فيما ينفعهم، لا فيما يضرهم، وقوله في أصل المسألة : دار" "رجل" مثال وليس بقيد، حتى لو مال العلو إلى السفل، أو الأسفل إلى العلو، فالحكم كذلك. (تكملة وغيره)
So the request is to its owner, i.e., the owner of the house, whether he is owner or tenant by lease or otherwise. The genitive in his saying "the house of a person" is for minimal relation, and its explanation is that the right belongs specifically to the owner of the house, so the request is entrusted to him especially, not to others. (Fath and others) "Valid, etc.": i.e., the owner of the house's discharge and deferment are valid, such that if it falls in the case of discharge or before the elapse of the period in the case of deferment, he is not liable. Contrary to when the wall leans to the public road: the judge defers it, and whoever requests demolition or discharge, the deferment and discharge are not valid except concerning his own right, because the right belongs to the Muslim community, and the judge's action concerning the general public is valid in what benefits them, not in what harms them. And his saying in the original issue: "the house of a man" is an example, not a restriction, such that even if the upper leans to the lower, or the lower to the upper, the ruling is likewise. (Takmila and others)
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof clarifying the procedure when a wall leans to a private house versus a public road. For a private house, the builder must request demolition from the house owner, who may discharge him from liability or grant a delay. If the wall falls after such discharge or within the delay period, the builder is not liable. For a public road, the request goes to the judge, and only the judge can validly intervene because the right belongs to all Muslims. The ruling applies similarly if one part of a building leans onto another part owned by someone else.

Key Terms
إبراء (ibrā’): Discharge from liability; the house owner waives his right to demand demolition.
تأجيل (ta’jīl): Deferment of the obligation to demolish the leaning wall for a specified period.
دار الإنسان (dār al-insān): The house of a person; used in the text as an example to denote private property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a wall leans to a public road, who may validly grant a delay (ta’jīl) for demolition?
A) The owner of the wall.
B) Any passerby.
C) The judge (qāḍī).
D) The owner of a neighboring private house.

Key Takeaway
The right to demand demolition belongs either to a private owner or the public, and only the respective right-holder (or the judge) can validly discharge or defer that obligation.

Rider Liability for Animal-Caused Damage
Defines the chapter and states the general liability of a rider for certain animal actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on a rider's liability for animal-caused damage and apply the distinction between controllable and uncontrollable actions.

Core Idea
A rider is liable for what his animal controls (treading, biting), not for uncontrollable kicks—unless he stops it.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqā'iq opens the chapter on animal injuries, laying down the foundational principle: a rider is liable for damages from his animal's predictable, controllable actions, but not for sudden kicks. The commentary expands this with a rational proof based on balancing rights on the public road.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What exactly is the ruling on the rider's liability? The matn states liability for trodding (by hand/leg/head), biting, and striking; no liability for kicking with leg or tail unless the animal is stopped.
Liability is for damage caused by the animal's actions.
2
Locate the dalil
What is the proof? The rational principle: passing in the road is permissible with the condition of safety in what can be guarded against. This is a dalil aqli (rational proof) based on balancing the rider's right to travel and the public's right to safety.
The text explicitly states this principle.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the principle lead to the ruling? Controllable actions (treading, biting, striking) can be guarded against, so liability attaches to ensure safety. Kicking while moving cannot be guarded against, so no liability. But stopping the animal is controllable, so liability arises when the rider stops and the animal kicks.
The key distinction is guardability (imkan al-ihtiraz).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Which schools disagree? Shafi'i and Ahmad (in one narration) hold the rider liable for kicks even without stoppage. Malik holds no liability for kicks at all, whether stopped or not.
Cross-madhhab dispute affects practical application.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Why is the Hanafi position relied upon? The text does not explicitly respond but the implicit istihsan is that imposing liability for uncontrollable actions would restrict permissible passage. The Hanafi view balances interests.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب جناية البهيمة والجناية عليها وغير ذلك
ضمن الراكب ما أوطات دابته بيد أو رجل أو رأس أو كدمت أو خبطت، لا ما نفحت برجل أو ذنب إلا إذا أوقفها في الطريق.
باب جناية إلخ : قال في "التكملة: لما فرغ به من بيان أحكام جناية الإنسان شرع في بيان جناية البهيمة، ولا شك في تقدم جناية الإنسان على البهيمة كذا في "النهاية"، ويرد عليه أنه لم يفرغ من بيان جناية الإنسان مطلقا بل بقي منها جناية المملوك، ولا شك أنه من الإنسان فيقدم على البهيمة، وكان من حقه أن يقدم على جناية البهيمة كذا في "غاية البيان". [تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۸۱/۹] وقال في "الفتح": عن أحمد بن يونس كان من حق هذا الباب أن يذكر بعد جناية المملوك لفضيلة النطق في المملوك، لكن لما كانت البهيمة ملحقة بالجماد من حيث عدم العقل، ألحق هذا الباب بباب ما يحدث الرجل في الطريق. البهيمة البهيمة كل ذات أربع قوائم ولو في البحر، والجمع بهائم. (فتح) ضمن الراكب إلخ: اعلم أن الأصل في مسائل هذا الباب: أن المرور في طريق المسلمين مباح بشرط السلامة فيما يمكن الاحتراز عنه؛ لأنه تصرف في حقه من وجه، وفي حق غيره من وجه؛ لكونه مشتركا بين كل الناس فقلنا بالإجازة مقيدا بشرط السلامة ليعتدل النظر الجانبين فيما يمكن الاحتراز لا فيما لا يمكن الاحتراز عنه؛ لأنه يؤدي إلى منع التصرف، والاحتراز عن الإيطاء والكدم والصدم والخبط ممكن؛ لأنه ليس من ضرورة السير، وقيدناه بشرط السلامة والكدم: العض بمقدم الأسنان، والخبط : الضرب باليد تكملة عيني رد المحتار لا ما نفحت إلخ أي لا يضمن الراكب ما نفحت دابته برجل أو ذنب؛ لأنه لا يمكنه الاحتراز عن النفحة بالرجل والذنب مع السير على الدابة فلم نقيده بالسلامة، كما الأصل يقال: نفحه برجله إذا ضربه وهو بالحاء المهملة من باب فعل يفعل بالفتح فيهما. رمز الحقائق: ٤٥٢/٢] إلا إذا أوقفها إلخ استثناء من قوله : "لا" ما نفحت" يعني لا يضمن في النفحة بالرجل والذنب إلا إذا أوقف الدابة في الطريق، فنفحت برجلها أو ذنبها فحينئذ يضمن؛ لأنه يمكنه الاحتراز عن الإيقاف، وإن لم يمكنه . فصار متعديا بالإيقاف فيضمنه، وعند الشافعي وأحمد في رواية: يضمن ما نفحت من غير توقيفه أيضا، وعند لا ضمان في فعل الرجل والذنب أوقف أو لم يوقف وباب المسجد كالطريق في الإيقاف، ولو جعل الإمام موضعا لوقوف الدواب عند باب المسجد، فلا ضمان فيما حدث من الوقوف، وكذا إيقاف الدابة في سوق الدواب؛ لأنه مأذون له من جهة السلطان وكذلك الفلاة وطريق مكة إذا أوقف في غير المحجة؛ لأنه لا يضر بالناس، فلا يحتاج فيه إلى الإذن، أما المحجة فهي كالطريق رمز الحقائق: ٤٥٢/١]
Chapter on injuries caused by animals and injuries upon them, and other matters. The rider is liable for what his animal tramples with hand or leg or head, or bites, or strikes; not for what it kicks with leg or tail, unless he stops it in the road. (Commentary) ... The rider is liable etc.: Know that the principle in the issues of this chapter is that passing in the Muslims' road is permissible with the condition of safety in what is possible to guard against, because it is a disposition in his right in one respect and in the right of others in another respect, since it is shared among all people. So we said the permission is restricted by the condition of safety so that consideration is balanced for both sides in what can be guarded against, not in what cannot be guarded against, because that would lead to preventing disposition. And guarding against trodding, biting, pushing, and striking is possible because it is not a necessary part of travel, so we restrict it with the condition of safety. Al-kadm: biting with the front teeth; al-khabt: striking with the hand. (Takmila, Ayni, Radd al-Muhtar) "Not what it kicks, etc.": i.e., the rider is not liable for what his animal kicks with leg or tail, because he cannot guard against the kick with leg and tail while riding the animal, so we did not restrict it with safety. (Ramz al-Haqā’iq) "Unless he stops it, etc.": exception from his saying "not what it kicks" – meaning he is not liable for the kick with leg or tail unless he stops the animal in the road, and it kicks with its leg or tail – then he is liable, because he can guard against stopping, even if he cannot guard against the kick itself. So he becomes a transgressor by stopping and is liable. According to al-Shafi‘i and Ahmad in one narration: he is liable for what it kicks even without his stopping it. And according to Malik: no liability for the action of leg or tail, whether he stops or not. And the area of the mosque is like the road regarding stopping. If the imam makes a place for stopping beasts by the mosque door, then there is no liability for what occurs from the stopping. Similarly, stopping an animal in the beast market, because he is permitted by the authority; likewise the open plain and the road to Mecca if he stops outside the beaten path, because it does not harm people, so permission is not needed. As for the beaten path, it is like the road. (Ramz al-Haqā’iq)
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof, opening the chapter on animal injuries. The matn states that the rider is liable for damage caused by his animal's trodding (using hand, leg, head), biting, and striking, because these can be guarded against while riding. However, he is not liable for kicking with leg or tail because this is uncontrollable during motion. The exception: if he stops the animal in a public road, then he is liable even for kicks, because he could have avoided stopping. The commentary elaborates the principle: passing in the road is a permissive act conditioned on safety where possible. The discussion compares with the views of Shafi‘i (liable for kicks without stopping), Ahmad (similar), and Malik (no liability for kicks at all). Special rulings are given for stopping near mosques, in markets, or on the open plain.

Key Terms
إيطاء (īṭā’): Trodding or trampling by the animal's feet.
كدم (kadm): Biting with the front teeth.
خبط (khabṭ): Striking with the hand or foreleg.
نفح (nafḥ): Kicking with the leg or tail; here used for backward kick.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is a rider liable for damage caused by his animal kicking with its leg?
A) Always, because the animal is under his control.
B) Never, because kicking is uncontrollable.
C) Only if he stopped the animal in the road before the kick.
D) Only if the animal kicked while being ridden.

Key Takeaway
A rider is liable for controllable animal actions (treading, biting, striking) but not for uncontrollable kicks unless he stops the animal—because the condition of safety applies only where guardability exists.

Slave Committing Two Crimes: Master's Options and Liability upon Freeing
Ruling on slave's two crimes and master's liability upon freeing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a slave who commits two crimes, explain the master's options, and distinguish the master's liability when freeing the slave with or without knowledge of the crime.

Core Idea
A master forfeits victims' rights by freeing the slave; he pays the lesser of value or arsh if unaware, and full arsh if aware.

This passage from the Hanafi fiqh commentary addresses the legal scenario of a slave who commits two separate crimes (jinaayatayn). The master is given a choice: surrender the slave to the victims or ransom him by paying the full compensation. A critical sub-issue arises when the master frees the slave without knowledge of the crimes—what then is his liability? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced answer that balances the rights of the victims and the master's ownership, differing from other schools. This lesson walks you through the ruling, its proof, and the points of disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic ruling
State the rule for a slave committing two crimes: the master may surrender the slave for both crimes or ransom him by paying their compensation (arsh).
The option belongs to the master, not the victims.
2
Understand the rationale
Each crime attaches to the slave's neck like successive debts to an insolvent estate; one does not prevent the other.
Analogy to successive debts (dayn mutalaahiq).
3
Analyze the sub-case: freeing without knowledge
If the master frees the slave unaware of the crime, he is liable for the lesser of the slave's value and the arsh (compensation).
The master forfeited the victims' right to surrender the slave.
4
Analyze the sub-case: freeing with knowledge
If the master frees the slave knowing of the crime, he is liable for the full arsh by consensus.
Freeing with knowledge constitutes a choice to ransom (ikhtiyar lil-fida).
5
Contrast with other schools
Al-Shafi'i (in one view) and Malik hold that in the without-knowledge case, the master is liable only for the arsh, not the lesser of value and arsh.
The practical difference arises when the slave's value is less than the arsh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن جنى جنايتين دفعه بهما أو فداه بأرشهما ، فإن أعتقه غير عالم بالجناية ضمن الأقل من قيمته ومن الأرش، ولو عالما بها لزمه الأرش كبيعه وتعليق عتقه بقتل فلان ورميه، وشجه إن فعل ذلك عبد قطع يد حر عمدا، ودفع إليه، فحرره فمات من اليد، فالعبد صلح بالجناية، وإن لم يحرره رده على سيده، والعبد مقطوع اليد، والمسألة بحالها أي العبد ورثة الحر .

وإن جنى الخ: يعني وإن جنى العبد جنايتين، فالمولى بالخيار إن شاء دفع العبد بالجنايتين، وإن شاء فداه بأرشهما؛ لأن تعلق الأولى برقبته لا يمنع تعلق الثانية بها كالديون المتلاحقة، ثم إذا دفعه إليهم اقتسموه على قدر حقوقهم، وحق كل واحد منهم أرش جنايته، وللمولى أن يفدي من بعضهم، ويأخذ نصيبه من العبد، ويدفع الباقي إلى غيره؛ لاختلاف الحقوق، بخلاف ما إذا كان المقتول واحدا وله وليان أو أولياء، حيث لم يكن له أن يفدي من البعض ويدفع الباقي إلى البعض لاتحاد الحق رمز الحقائق: ٤٥٧/٢] دفعه بهما: أي دفع المولى العبد بالجنايتين إن شاء. فإن أعتقه إلخ يعني إن أعتق المولى عبده وهو غير عالم بالجناية، ضمن المولى الأقل من قيمته، والأقل من الأرش؛ لأنه فوت حقه بما صنع، فيضمنه، وحقه في أقلهما، وعند الشافعي في قول ومالك ضمن الأرش فقط رمز الحقائق: ٤٥٧/٢] ولو عالما بها إلخ أي ولو أعتقه عالما بها لزمه الأرش فقط بالإجماع؛ لأنه صار مختارا للفداء بهذا العتق؛ لأن الإعتاق يمنع من الدفع والإقدام عليه اختيار فإذا أعتقه وهو يعلم بالجناية صار مختارا للفداء. [تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٠٧/٩] :كبيعه أي كما لزمه الأرش فقط في بيعه العبد الجاني حال كونه عالما بالجناية، وإطلاق البيع ينتظم البيع بشرط الخيار للمشتري لا للبائع، ولو باعه فاسدا لا يصير مختارا إلا بالتسليم ولو باعه من المجني عليه فهو مختار للفداء، بخلاف الهبة منه، وإذا باعه غير عالم لزمه الأقل، كما في العتق، فلو قال: فإن أعتقه أو باعه غير عالم كان أولى وإعتاق المجني عليه بأمر المولى كإعتاق المولى فيما ذكرناه، ومثل البيع الهبة والتدبير والاستيلاد. رمز الحقائق: ٤٥٧/٢] (طائي) وتعليق عتقه إلخ بالجر عطف على قوله : "كبيعه" أي وكتعليق عتقه، يعني وكما يلزمه الأرش فقط بتعليق عتق العبد بقتل فلان بأن قال: إن قتلت فلانا فأنت حر، وبرمي فلان بأن قال له: إن رميت فلانا فأنت حر، وبشج فلان بأن قال: إن شججت فلانا فأنت حر، إن فعل العبد ذلك أي القتل والرمي والشج، وفي تعليق العتق خلاف زفر، فعنده: لا يصير مختارا للفداء، وعليه القيمة؛ لأنه لم يوجد الاختيار بعد الجناية، ولنا أن تعليق العتق مع . علمه بأنه يعتق عند القتل دليل اختياره، فتلزمه الدية [ رمز الحقائق: ٤٥٧/٢] ودفع إليه : أي إلى الحر الذي قطع يده. فالعبد صلح إلخ لأنه قصد صحة الإعتاق ولا صحة له إلا بالصلح عن الجناية وما يحدث منها ابتداء، ولهذا لو نص عليه، ورضي به، جاز، وكان مصالحا عن الجناية، وما يحدث منها رمز الحقائق: ٤٥٨/٢] واعلم أن فائدة التقييد بالعمد لا يظهر في ما حرره بل يظهر في ما إذا لم يحرره، ظاهر، فذكر قيد العمد في أول المسألة توطئة؛ لما سيأتي من قوله : 'وإن لم يحرره' إلخ. (فتح)
If he commits two crimes, he is surrendered for them or ransomed for their compensation. If he frees him unaware of the crime, he is liable for the lesser of his value and the compensation; if aware, the compensation is incumbent, like his sale and suspension of his emancipation upon killing so-and-so, throwing [a spear], and fracturing [his skull], if the slave does that—a slave who intentionally cut a free man's hand, and he was handed over to him, and he freed him, and he died from the hand, then the slave is settled by the crime; if he does not free him, he returns him to his master. The slave is the one with the amputated hand, and the case remains—meaning the slave's heirs [are] the free man. 

‘If he commits...’ etc.: meaning if the slave commits two crimes, the master has the option: if he wishes, he surrenders the slave for both crimes; if he wishes, he ransoms him with their compensation, because the attachment of the first to his neck does not prevent the attachment of the second, like successive debts. Then if he surrenders him to them, they divide him according to their shares, and each one's share is the compensation for his crime. The master may ransom from some and take his portion of the slave, and deliver the remainder to another, due to the difference of rights, unlike when the slain is one and has two or more guardians, where he may not ransom from some and deliver the remainder to others due to the unity of the right. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 2/457] ‘Surrenders him for them’: meaning the master surrenders the slave for both crimes if he wishes. ‘If he frees him...’ etc.: meaning if the master frees his slave while unaware of the crime, the master is liable for the lesser of his value and the lesser of the compensation, because he has forfeited his right by what he has done, so he is liable for it, and his right is in the lesser of the two. According to al-Shafi'i in one opinion and Malik, he is only liable for the compensation. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 2/457] ‘And if aware...’ etc.: meaning if he freed him knowingly, the compensation alone is incumbent by consensus, because he becomes a chooser of ransom by this emancipation; for emancipation prevents surrender and embarking upon it is choice. So if he frees him knowing of the crime, he becomes a chooser of ransom. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Raiq: 9/207] ‘Like his sale’: meaning the compensation alone is incumbent in the sale of the offending slave while the master is aware of the crime. The absolute sale includes a sale with a condition of option for the buyer, not the seller. If he sells it in a corrupt sale, he does not become a chooser except upon delivery. If he sells it to the victim, he is a chooser of ransom, unlike a gift from him. If he sells it unaware, the lesser is incumbent, as in emancipation. So it would be better if he said: ‘if he frees or sells him while unaware’—and the emancipation by the victim at the master's command is like the master's emancipation in what we mentioned. Similar to sale are gift, tadbir, and istilad. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 2/457] (Tyai) ‘And suspension of his emancipation...’ etc. is in the genitive case, coordinated with ‘like his sale’: meaning and like the suspension of his emancipation. So the compensation alone is incumbent by suspending the slave's emancipation upon killing so-and-so, as in ‘if you kill so-and-so, you are free’, and upon throwing so-and-so, and upon fracturing so-and-so, if the slave does that—the killing, throwing, and fracturing. There is a difference from Zufar regarding suspension of emancipation: according to him, he does not become a chooser of ransom, and the value is upon him, because choice did not exist after the crime. Our proof is that suspending emancipation with his knowledge that he will be freed upon killing is evidence of his choice, so the blood-money is incumbent. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 2/457] ‘And he is handed over to him’: meaning to the free man whose hand he cut. ‘Then the slave is settled...’ etc. because he intended the validity of emancipation, and it is only valid through settlement of the crime and what arises from it initially. Therefore, if he stipulated it and agreed, it is permissible, and he becomes a settler of the crime and what arises from it. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 2/458] Know that the benefit of restricting it to the intentional injury is not apparent in what he freed, but rather in what he did not free—it is obvious. So he mentioned the restriction of being intentional at the beginning of the issue as a preamble for what comes later, as in his statement ‘and if he does not free him’, etc. (Fath).
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school regarding a slave who commits multiple crimes and the master's options. The author presents a complex legal scenario and the commentator expands on it with reasoning and differences. 

First, the ruling: If a slave commits two crimes, the master can either hand over the slave for both crimes—in which case the victims divide the slave proportionally to their compensation—or ransom the slave by paying the full compensation for both crimes. This is because each crime attaches to the slave's value, similar to successive debts attaching to an insolvent estate. The master may even ransom from some victims and keep his share, due to the independence of each right. 

A critical sub-case arises when the master frees the slave without knowing of the crimes. In that case, the master is liable for the lesser of two amounts: the slave's value and the total compensation (arsh). This is because the master forfeited the victims' rights by freeing the slave, and his liability is limited to the minimum that could satisfy either right (value or arsh). The Shafi'i school (in one view) and Malik hold that the master is liable only for the compensation, not the value—a difference in principle. 

If the master frees the slave with knowledge of the crimes, he is liable for the full compensation by consensus (ijma), because his act constitutes a choice to ransom the slave rather than surrender him. This principle extends analogically (qiyas) to sale, gift, tadbir, and istilad: if the master disposes of the slave while aware of the crime, he is liable for the compensation; if unaware, the lesser of value and compensation. 

A further sub-case involves a slave who cuts a free man's hand intentionally, and the victim frees the slave. If the victim later dies from the injury, the liquidation of the slave's value via settlement (sulh) for the hand is retroactively invalid because the true liability becomes retaliation (qisas). Since qisas does not apply between a free man and a slave for limbs, the settlement was void and the slave is subject to qisas. The commentator notes that the qualification 'intentional' in the original matn serves as a preamble for this scenario. 

The passage also records an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Zufar holds that conditional emancipation (e.g., 'if you kill, you are free') does not make the master a chooser of ransom, so he is liable for the slave's value, not the compensation. The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa) is that such a conditional is evidence of choice, so the master bears the blood-money. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) is the Hanafi view as per the text.

Key Terms
جناية (jinaaya): A crime or injury committed by one person against another, specifically requiring compensation or retaliation.
أرش (arsh): Monetary compensation for an injury (e.g., for a limb) where the amount is not fixed by Quran or Hadith but determined by the value of the part injured.
دفع (daf'): Surrendering the slave to the victim(s) to satisfy their right to compensation or retaliation.
فداء (fida'): Ransoming the slave by paying the full compensation to avoid surrender.
صلح (sulh): A settlement agreement between the parties to resolve the liability, here used for the compromise when the slave is freed and the victim dies.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a slave commits two crimes, and the master frees him without knowing about either crime, what is the master's liability?
A) The master is liable for the full compensation (arsh) of both crimes.
B) The master is liable for the lesser of the slave's value and the total arsh.
C) The master is not liable for anything because he was unaware.
D) The master must surrender the slave to the victims.

Key Takeaway
When a master frees a slave unaware of a crime, he pays the lesser of the slave's value and the arsh; with knowledge, he pays the full arsh.

The Master's Dual Liability When Freeing an Indebted, Offending Ma'dhun Slave
Ruling on the master's liability for two values

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a master frees an indebted, offending ma'dhun slave without knowledge, and apply the conditions that determine whether the master owes one value, two values, or the full debt and arsh.

Core Idea
A master freeing an indebted, offending ma'dhun slave without knowledge owes two separate values: one to the creditor and one to the victim.

This passage addresses a nuanced scenario: a ma'dhun slave (permitted to trade) incurs debt and then commits a mistake crime. The master, unaware of the debt and crime, frees the slave. The Hanafi school prescribes a dual liability because the master's act destroys two independent rights—the creditor's right to sell the slave and the victim's right to surrender him. Understanding this ruling requires analyzing the nature of these rights and the conditions that modify the liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the parties
Recognize that the act is the master freeing his ma'dhun slave who has both an existing debt and a liability from a mistake crime (jinaaya khata'). The master does so without knowledge of these obligations.
The slave is ma'dhun, so his debts are valid; the crime is mistake, not intentional, so liability is financial (arsh).
2
Determine the rights destroyed
Two distinct rights were attached to the slave: (1) the creditor's right to have the slave sold for the debt, and (2) the victim's (or his heirs') right to have the slave surrendered as compensation for the crime. Each right is guaranteed by the entire value of the slave individually.
In normal circumstances, if the slave were not freed, he would be surrendered to the victim first, then sold for the debt, with any surplus to the victim.
3
Apply the principle of dual guarantee
Since the master himself destroyed both rights by freeing the slave, he must compensate each right separately. Thus he owes the slave's value to the creditor and the slave's value to the victim—two values, not one. This is analogous to a situation where an outsider destroys the slave, but in that case only one value is due to the master (who then pays the creditors), because the outsider's liability is limited to ownership.
The dual liability is conditioned on the slave's value being less than both the debt and the arsh. If the value is greater or equal to both, the master pays the debt and arsh in full.
4
Consider the effect of knowledge
If the master frees the slave with knowledge of the debt and crime, his liability changes: he owes the slave's value to the creditor and the arsh (not the value) to the victim. This is because knowing emancipation is akin to deliberately causing loss to the rights-holders.
The passage also notes that if the master does not free the slave, he has the option to surrender him (for sale and surrender) or ransom him, per the general principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقاد. جنى مأذون مديون خطأ، فحرره سیده بلا علم عليه قيمته لرب الدين، وقيمته لولي الجناية. مأذونة مديونة ولدت بيعت مع ولدها للدين، وإن جنت فولدت لم يدفع الولد له. عبد زعم رجل أن سيده حرره

ويقاد: أي يقتص العبد; لأنه ظهر أن الصلح كان باطلا؛ لأنه وقع على المال وهو العبد عن دية اليد؛ إذ القصاص لا يجري بين الحر والعبد في الأطراف، وبالسراية ظهر أن دية اليد غير واجبة، وأن الواجب هو القود، فصار الصلح باطلا؛ لأن الصلح لا بد له من مصالح عنه والمصالح عنه المال ولم يوجد فبطل الصلح، والباطل لا يورث شبهة، كما لو وطئ مطلقته ثلاثا في عدتها مع العلم بحرمتها عليه، فإنه لا يصير شبهة في درء الحد، فكذا هذا، فوجب القصاص، فالأولياء بالخيار إن شاءوا عفوا عنه، وإن شاءوا قتلوه [ رمز الحقائق: ٤٥٨/٢] (تكملة) فحرره إلخ: وإن لم يحرره فهو مخير، والأصل أن العبد إذا جنى وعليه دين خير المولى بين الدفع والفداء، فإن اختار الدفع، بيع في دين الغرماء، فإن فضل شيء كان لأصحاب الجناية؛ لأنه بيع على ملكهم، وإن لم يف بالدين تأخر إلى حال الحرية. (رد المحتار) فلا عليه قيمته إلخ: أي يجب على السيد قيمتان: قيمة لرب الدين، وقيمة أخرى لولي الجناية؛ لأنه أتلف حقين كل واحد منهما مضمون بكل القيمة على الانفراد الدفع للأولياء والبيع للغرماء، فكذا عند الاجتماع؛ لعدم التزاحم بينهما؛ إذ لو لا الإعتاق لدفع إلى ولي الجناية ثم يباع للدين فإن فضل شيء عن ديونهم كان لولي الجناية، وإلا شيء له، بخلاف ما إذا أتلفه أجنبي حيث تحب قيمة واحدة للمولى، ويدفعها المولى للغرماء؛ لأن الأجنبي إنما يضمن للمولى بحكم الملك، فلا يظهر في مقابلة الحق أي حق الدفع؛ لأنه دونه، وهنا يجب كل منهما بإتلاف الحق أي الدفع للأولياء والبيع للغرماء، فيضمنهما، ثم وجوب القيمتين عليه مقيد بما إذا كانت القيمة أقل من الدين، أو من الأرش، وإن كانت أكثر يجب الدين والأرش، وقيد المسألة بعدم العلم؛ لأنه لو أعتقه بعد العلم، فعليه قيمته لرب الدين، وأرش الجناية لأولياء المجني عليه. (فتح، ملا مسكين) ولدت إلخ: أي من غير مولاها؛ لأنها لو ولدت منه تصير أم ولد له فلا تباع ولا تدفع لولي الجناية. (فتح) لم يدفع إلخ والفرق بين هذه الصورة وبين الأولى، حيث يباع الولد في الدين، ولا يدفع في الجناية: أن الدين وصف حكمي فيها واجب في ذمتها متعلق برقبتها، فيسري إلى الولد كولد المرهونة، بخلاف الجناية؛ لأن موجب الجناية الدفع في ذمة المولى لا في ذمتها، فلا يسري إلى الولد. واعلم أن ما ذكره المصنف هو ظاهر الرواية، =
And he is executed retaliation. A ma'dhun slave who incurred debt and committed a crime by mistake, and his master freed him without knowledge, is liable for his value to the debtor and his value to the guardian of the crime. A ma'dhuna female slave who is indebted and gives birth is sold with her child for the debt; if she commits a crime and then gives birth, the child is not surrendered for him. A slave about whom a man claims that his master freed him.

‘And he is executed retaliation’: meaning the slave is retaliated, because it becomes clear that the settlement was invalid; it was based on property (the slave) for the hand's diya, but retaliation does not apply between a free man and a slave for limbs, and by transmission it becomes clear that the hand's diya is not obligatory, but rather the obligatory is qisas, so the settlement is invalid. The settlement must have a subject matter for settlement, but the subject matter was property and it did not exist, so it is void. An invalid matter does not create a doubt, just as if one has intercourse with his thrice-divorced wife during her idda knowing its prohibition, that does not create a doubt to avert the hadd; likewise here, so qisas is obligatory. The guardians have the option: if they wish, they pardon him; if they wish, they kill him. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 2/458] (Takmila) ‘If he frees him...’ etc.: if he does not free him, he has choice. The principle is that if a slave commits a crime and has a debt, the master has the choice between surrender and ransom. If he chooses surrender, he (the slave) is sold for the creditors' debt; if anything remains, it goes to the crime claimants, because the sale is on their ownership. If it does not cover the debt, it is postponed until freedom. (Radd al-Muhtar) ‘Then upon him his value...’ etc.: meaning two values are incumbent on the master: a value for the debtor and another value for the guardian of the crime, because he has destroyed two rights, each of which is guaranteed by the entire value individually—surrender to the guardians and sale for the creditors—so likewise in combination, because there is no conflict between them; if not for the emancipation, he would have been surrendered to the guardian of the crime, then sold for the debt, and if anything remained after the debts, it would go to the guardian of the crime; otherwise nothing for him. This differs from if a third party destroys him, where one value is taken for the master and paid by the master to the creditors, because the third party is only liable to the master by virtue of ownership; so it does not appear in opposition to the right of surrender, because it is less than that. Here each is obligatory by destroying the right, i.e., surrender to the guardians and sale for the creditors, so he guarantees both. Then the obligation of two values is conditioned on the value being less than the debt or the arsh; if it is more, the debt and arsh are obligatory. The issue is conditioned on lack of knowledge; because if he freed him after knowledge, then upon him is his value to the debtor and the arsh of the crime to the guardians of the victim. (Fath, Mulla Miskeen) ‘Gives birth...’ etc.: meaning from other than her master, because if she gave birth from him, she becomes an umm walad and cannot be sold or surrendered to the guardian of the crime. (Fath) ‘The child is not surrendered...’ etc. The difference between this case and the first, where the child is sold for the debt and not surrendered for the crime, is that debt is a legal attribute upon her, obligatory in her dhimmah and attached to her neck, so it extends to the child like the child of a mortgaged slave; unlike the crime, because the obligation of the crime is surrender in the master's dhimmah, not in hers, so it does not extend to the child. Know that what the author mentioned is the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya).
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage contains multiple rulings concerning a ma'dhun (permitted to trade) slave who is indebted and commits a crime, as well as a female slave in similar circumstances. It also continues the previous discussion regarding retaliation when a settlement is invalid.

First, the passage states that a ma'dhun slave who incurs debt and commits a crime by mistake, and the master frees him without knowledge, is liable for two values: one to the debtor (creditor) and one to the guardian of the victim (for the crime). This is because the master has destroyed two rights: the creditors' right to sell the slave and the victim's right to surrender him. The slave's value is shared between these rights proportionally.

However, if the master does not free the slave, he has the option to surrender him (which leads to sale for debt first, then remainder to victim) or ransom. This is the general principle.

A key distinction is made: if an outsider (not the master) destroys the slave, only one value is due to the master, who then pays the creditors. But here, the master himself destroys both rights, so two separate values are due. The commentator clarifies that this dual liability is conditioned on the slave's value being less than the debt and the arsh; if it exceeds them, the debt and arsh are paid in full. Also, the ruling applies only when the master frees without knowledge; if he frees with knowledge, he owes the value to the debtor and the arsh to the victim—not two values.

Second, the passage addresses a female ma'dhun slave (madhuna) who has debt and gives birth. She is sold with her child to satisfy the debt. But if she commits a crime after the debt and then gives birth, the child is not surrendered for the crime. The difference is that debt is a legal attribute that attaches to her and extends to her child (like a mortgaged slave's child), whereas the crime's obligation is on the master's dhimmah, not on her, so it does not extend to the child.

Finally, the passage includes a brief note about a slave whom a man claims his master freed—this likely refers to a dispute over freedom, but no further elaboration is given here.

The commentator relates these rulings to the earlier case of a slave who cut a free man's hand intentionally, was freed, and the victim died. The settlement (sulh) was invalid because the liability turned into qisas upon death, and thus the slave is executed (qisas). The guardians may pardon or demand execution. This is the meaning of 'wa yuqad' (and he is executed).

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave who has been given permission by his master to engage in trade, which allows him to incur debts related to his business.
قيمته (qimatu): The market value of the slave at the time of the event, used as the measure of liability.
ولي الجناية (wali al-jinaaya): The guardian of the victim of the crime (the person or his heirs) entitled to compensation or retaliation.
رب الدين (rabb al-dayn): The creditor to whom the slave owes a debt.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The transmitted rulings from Imam Muhammad's six major works (Zahir al-Riwayah), which are the most authoritative within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What condition triggers the master's liability for two separate values when freeing an indebted, offending ma'dhun slave without knowledge?
A) The slave's value must be less than both the debt and the arsh.
B) The slave's value must be greater than both the debt and the arsh.
C) The master must have freed the slave with knowledge.
D) The slave must have committed an intentional crime.

Key Takeaway
A master who frees an indebted, offending ma'dhun slave without knowledge owes two values, one to the creditor and one to the victim, because he has destroyed two independent rights.

The Child of an Indebted Ma'dhuna Female Slave: Debt vs. Crime
Ruling on the child of an indebted female slave who commits a crime

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the effect of debt and crime on the child of a ma'dhuna female slave, and explain why the child is sold for the debt but not surrendered for the crime.

Core Idea
A female slave's child is sold with her to satisfy her debt, but not surrendered for her crime because debt attaches to her person, while crime liability is on her master.

This passage presents two scenarios involving a ma'dhuna female slave (a slave woman permitted to trade, called ma'dhuna) who is indebted and also commits a crime. When she gives birth, the child's fate depends on the nature of the obligation: debt or crime. The Hanafi ruling makes a sharp distinction based on whether the liability is attached to her own person or to her master's dhimmah. Understanding this distinction is key to applying the rules of slavery and liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Scenario A: The female slave has a debt and gives birth (child is born after debt incurred). Scenario B: The female slave has a debt, commits a crime, and then gives birth (child born after both debt and crime).
In both scenarios, the child is born from someone other than her master, because if from the master, she becomes an umm walad and cannot be sold.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
In Scenario A: The female slave and her child are sold together to satisfy the debt. In Scenario B: The female slave is sold for the debt, but the child is not surrendered to the victim for the crime.
The child remains with the mother in sale but is not handed over for jinaaya.
3
Understand the legal rationale
Debt is a 'legal attribute' (wasf hukmi) that is obligatory in her dhimmah and attached to her neck (raqabah). It extends to her child just like the child of a mortgaged slave is also mortgaged. Crime liability, however, is a right of surrender (daf') that is on the master's dhimmah, not on the female slave herself. Therefore, it does not extend to her child.
The analogy: the child of a mortgaged slave is also subject to the mortgage; similarly, the child of a female slave with debt is subject to the debt.
4
Apply the distinction to other cases
If the female slave had only a crime liability (no debt), and she gives birth, the child is not surrendered. If she had only debt and gives birth, the child is sold. If she has both, the child is sold for debt but not for crime.
The child is always sold with the mother for debt, but never surrendered for a crime committed before the birth.

Compensation for a Usurped Slave: Full Value Due by Consensus
Ruling on full value for a usurped slave without reduction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on compensation for a usurped slave that perishes, and distinguish it from the ruling on unintentional killing of a slave.

Core Idea
Usurpation is a property tort – full market value is due without cap.

In this lesson, we examine a concise but powerful ruling from the Hanafi school: if someone usurps a slave (or any property) and it perishes, the full value must be paid, regardless of amount. This ruling is rooted in the nature of ghasb as a property transgression, not a personal injury. We will unpack the rule, its consensus basis, and how it differs from cases where a cap applies, such as diyah for killing a slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the obligation
Read the matn: 'wa fil-maghsoobi tajibu qeematuhu ma balaghat' – 'and regarding the usurped (slave): its value is obligatory whatever it reaches.' The act is ghasb (usurpation) of a slave; the obligation is full market value at the time of loss.
The value is assessed at the time of perishing, according to the commentary.
2
Identify the proof of obligation
The commentary states this ruling is by consensus (ijma') because ghasb pertains to property (al-ghasbu yaridu 'alal mal). The obligation is in exchange for the property itself, not for the human element. Hence no cap.
Ijma' is a definitive proof (qat'i) in Hanafi usul.
3
Distinguish from similar cases
Contrast with unintentional killing of a slave (qatl khata'), where the diyah is capped (e.g., 10,000 dirhams) because it involves a human life. Ghasb is purely property-based, so no reduction applies.
The same reasoning applies to any usurped property: full value without cap.

Source Text

وفي المغصوب تجب قيمته ما بلغت. وفي المغصوب إلخ: أي لو غصب عبدا أو أمة، وقيمته عشرون ألفا، فهلك عند الغاصب، تجب قيمته بالغة ما بلغت بالإجماع؛ لأن الغصب يرد على المال، فكان الواجب بمقابلة المال، فيجب بالغا ما بلغ. (ملا مسكين، عيني)
And regarding the usurped (slave): its value is obligatory whatever it reaches. (Commentary: 'And regarding the usurped, etc.': that is, if someone usurps a male or female slave and its value is twenty thousand, and it perishes in the usurper's possession, its value is obligatory whatever it reaches by consensus, because usurpation pertains to property, so the obligation is in exchange for property, so it is obligatory whatever it reaches. (Mulla Miskin, 'Ayni))
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and its commentary on the compensation for a usurped slave (maghsūb). The matn states that if a slave is wrongfully taken (ghasb) and perishes, the full value is due without any reduction. The commentary explains that this is by consensus (ijma') because ghasb is a property-based tort; the obligation is in exchange for the property (the slave's market value), not for the human aspect. Therefore, unlike the case of killing by mistake where the diyah cap applies due to the human element, here the full market value must be paid regardless of amount. The scholars (Mulla Miskin and al-'Ayni) confirm this ruling. This distinguishes between the two scenarios: unintentional killing (human aspect, capped) and usurpation (property aspect, full value).

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Wrongful taking and possession of another's property without permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if someone usurps a slave worth 100,000 dirhams and it perishes, how much is the compensation?
A) 9,990 dirhams
B) 10,000 dirhams
C) 100,000 dirhams
D) 50,000 dirhams

Key Takeaway
Full value is due for a usurped slave because ghasb is a property tort, and the obligation is in exchange for the property without cap.

Master's Retaking of Possession Frees the Ghasib from Liability
Ruling: master's act of cutting while slave in ghasib's possession retakes possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when the owner retakes possession of ghasb property through physical dominion and apply the qiyas to analogous cases.

Core Idea
Owner's physical retaking ends ghasb liability even if property perishes.

We now examine a second scenario where the master cuts the slave's hand while the slave is already in the ghasib's wrongful possession. Unlike the previous case where the ghasb occurred after the cut, here the cut happens during the ghasb. The text rules that the ghasib is free from liability, reasoning that the master's act of cutting is itself a retaking of possession (istirdad). This lesson unpacks the reasoning, including a qiyas on sale, and highlights the key distinction between the two cases.

Guided Path
1
State the Ruling
Read the first sentence: the ghasib is free from liability for the slave's death (due to the cut). Note this is the outcome.
2
Identify the Legal Cause (Illah)
The text gives the reason: the master, by cutting, becomes a retaker (mustarrid) because he exercises dominion (istila') over the slave. This retaking ends the ghasb and returns ownership to the master.
3
Trace the Qiyas on Sale
The author draws an analogy: if a buyer purchases an already‑amputated slave and the slave dies, the buyer bears the loss because the sale severs causation. Here, the master's cut is like the buyer's possession – it separates the injury from the death. But in the second case, there is no such separator because the cut occurs during the ghasb, so the master's act becomes the retaking.
The qiyas is based on the principle that ghasb is a cause of ownership like sale (قاطع للسراية كالبيع).
4
Apply the Ruling to the Scenario
Confirm: since the master's cut is a retaking, the ghasib is not responsible for the death. The liability shifts to the master because ownership has returned to him.
5
Distinguish from the Previous Case
In the previous case (not quoted here), the ghasb occurred after the cut, so the ghasb severed causation and the ghasib was liable. Here, the cut happens during the ghasb, so no severance occurs; instead, the master's act retakes possession.
The key difference is the timing of the ghasb relative to the cut.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قطع يده في يد الغاصب، فمات منه برئ الغاصب؛ لأن المولى لما قطع يده في يد الغاصب صار مستردا له؛ لاستيلائه عليه، وبرئ الغاصب من ضمانه؛ لوصول ملكه إلى يده وجه الفرق بين المسألتين: أن الغصب قاطع للسراية؛ لأنه سبب الملك كالبيع، فيصير كأنه هلك بآفة سماوية، فتجب قيمته أقطع، ألا ترى أنه لو باعه بعد ما قطع يده، فمات في يد المشتري، فإنه يموت من مال المشتري؛ لأن قبض المشتري صار فاصلا بين القطع والهلاك، فكذا هذا، ولم يوجد القاطع في الفصل الثاني، فصار المولى بقطع يده مستردا، فبرئ الغاصب من الضمان. (تكملة، فتح)
And if he cuts his hand while it is in the ghasib's hand, and it dies from it, the ghasib is free (from liability); because when the master cuts his hand while it is in the ghasib's hand, he becomes a retaker of it due to his dominion over it, and the ghasib is freed from his liability because his ownership reached his hand. The difference between the two cases: ghasb severs causation because it is a cause of ownership like sale, so it becomes as if it perished by a heavenly affliction, so his value as amputee is due. Do you not see that if he sold it after cutting its hand, and it died in the hand of the buyer, then it dies from the buyer's property, because the buyer's possession became a separator between the cut and death. So here too. And the separator is absent in the second case, so the master by cutting its hand becomes a retaker, so the ghasib is freed from liability. (Takmila, Fath)
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This is the second sub-ruling, contrasting with the previous one. Here, the master cuts the slave's hand while the slave is already in the wrongful possession of the ghasib. The ghasib is freed from any liability for the slave's subsequent death from the cut. The reasoning: by cutting the hand, the master exercises dominion (istila') over the slave, effectively retaking possession (istirdad). Since the master's ownership returns, the ghasib is no longer responsible. The commentary explains the difference between the two cases: in the first case, the ghasb occurred after the cut, so the ghasb severs the causal chain, making the ghasib liable. In this second case, there is no such separator because the cut happens during ghasb; instead, the master's act is itself a retaking. This illustrates the Hanafi rule that the owner's physical control overrides the ghasib's wrongful possession for liability purposes.

Key Terms
استرداد (istirdad): Retaking possession by the owner; here, the master's act of cutting is treated as a retaking.
استيلاء (istila'): Physical domination or control.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the ghasib freed from liability when the master cuts the slave's hand during the ghasb?
A) Because the cut causes the slave's value to decrease.
B) Because the master's act is considered a retaking of the slave from the ghasib.
C) Because the ghasib did not cause the cut.
D) Because the master still owns the slave.

Key Takeaway
The owner's physical act of dominion over property in ghasb constitutes retaking (istirdad), terminating the ghasb and freeing the ghasib from liability for subsequent harm.

Liability of a Confined Slave for Ghasb of Another Confined Slave
Ruling with conditional enforcement based on proof type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a confined slave wrongfully takes another confined slave who dies, and distinguish the enforcement of liability based on confession versus evidence.

Core Idea
A confined slave is liable for wrongfully taking another slave, but payment is deferred if proven by confession and immediate if proven by evidence.

This lesson examines a specific case in Hanafi fiqh: what happens when a slave under confinement (mahjur) wrongfully takes possession of another confined slave, and the latter dies in the taker's hand. The ruling imposes liability, but the method of proof affects how the compensation is collected. Understanding this nuance is crucial for grasping the Hanafi approach to the legal capacity of slaves and the interplay between confession and evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and action
Both are mahjur slaves; the act is ghasb (wrongful taking); the outcome is the death of the taken slave in the taker's possession.
2
State the ruling
The ghasib is liable (ضمان) for the value of the deceased slave.
3
Understand the legal basis
A confined person (mahjur) is still accountable for his harmful acts (jinayat), so he must compensate.
4
Recognize the enforcement distinction
If ghasb is proven by confession (iqrar), payment is deferred until the slave becomes free. If proven by evidence (bayyina), the slave is sold immediately to pay the debt.
The reason: a slave's confession cannot force a sale of his person, but clear evidence can.
5
Apply the rationale
This distinction reflects Hanafi rules on the legal capacity of slaves: their iqrar is accepted but does not trigger immediate execution against their person.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غصب محجور مثله فمات في يده ضمن. يعني إذا غصب عبد محجور عليه عبدا محجورا عليه فمات المغصوب في يد الغاصب، ضمنه؛ لأن المحجور عليه مؤاخذ بأفعاله، وهذا منها فيضمن تكملة البحر الرائق: ۲۳۷/۹] (عيني) وقال في "ملا مسكين" و"الفتح": ضمن الغاصب قيمته، ولكن يؤدي بعد العتق؛ لأن الفرض ثبوت الغصب بالإقرار، حتى لوثبت بالبينة يباع فيه للحال.
If a confined (mahjur) (slave) wrongfully takes another like him (a confined slave) and he dies in his hand, he is liable. i.e., if a slave who is under interdiction (mahjur 'alayh) wrongfully takes a slave who is also under interdiction, and the taken slave dies in the hand of the taker, he is liable for him; because the person under interdiction is held accountable for his actions, and this is from among them, so he is liable. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, 9/237) (Ayni) And it is said in "Mulla Miskin" and "Fath": The ghasib is liable for its value, but it (the payment) is deferred until after his emancipation; because the assumption is that the ghasb is proven by confession (iqrar), so that if it were proven by evidence (bayyina), he would be sold for it immediately.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage addresses a case where a slave under confinement (mahjur) wrongfully takes possession of another confined slave, and the latter dies in the possession of the taker. The ruling: the taker (ghasib) is liable for the value of the deceased slave. The reason is that a confined person (mahjur 'alayh) is still legally accountable for his tortious acts (jinayat). Therefore, he must pay compensation. However, there is a nuance regarding payment: if the ghasb is established by the confession (iqrar) of the slave, then the payment is deferred until after he becomes free (i.e., upon his master's death or manumission), because the slave's confession cannot force a sale of his person. But if the ghasb is proven by clear evidence (bayyina), then the slave can be sold immediately to satisfy the debt. This distinction reflects the Hanafi rule that a slave's confession is not binding for immediate sale, but evidence is.

Key Terms
محجور عليه (mahjur 'alayh): A person under interdiction, here a slave who is restricted from certain actions due to his status.
إقرار (iqrar): Confession, admission. Used here as the basis for proving the ghasb.
بينة (bayyina): Clear evidence, testimony. Proof by bayyina allows immediate sale of the slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the enforcement method if a confined slave's ghasb is proven by his own confession?
A) He is sold immediately to pay the value.
B) Payment is deferred until he becomes free.
C) The master is required to pay.
D) The slave is punished but no payment is required.

Key Takeaway
A confined slave is liable for wrongful taking of another slave, but the enforcement differs based on the type of proof: confession defers payment, evidence allows immediate sale.
